8 min
Rulings Change When Qiyas Is Based on Customary 'Illa
Explaining the second principle with the dyeing example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the illa of a qiyas is custom-based and derive the appropriate ruling when the custom changes.

Core Idea
If the cause of an analogy is itself customary, the ruling changes when the custom changes.

This lesson explores a deeper nuance: when the effective cause (illa) of an analogical ruling is rooted in custom, any change in that custom requires a corresponding change in the ruling. This is not a departure from qiyas but a logical extension of it. The example of dyeing a usurped garment illustrates how a change in what is considered a defect (based on custom) alters the legal options of the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas and its illa
First, identify the analogical ruling and the illa (cause) that connects it to the original case. For example, dyeing a garment black is considered a defect (ayb) by Abu Hanifa.
The illa here is based on the customary understanding of what constitutes a defect.
2
Determine if the illa is custom-based
Check whether the illa derives from a customary practice or understanding. If the custom defines what is a defect, then the illa is custom-based.
In the example, the custom of the time considered dyeing as a defect.
3
Assess if the custom has changed
If the custom has changed (e.g., dyeing may now be considered an improvement), the illa has effectively changed.
Qadi Ismail noted that customs change over time and place.
4
Apply the new custom to the illa and ruling
With the changed custom, the illa no longer applies, so the ruling must change. For example, if dyeing is now valued, the owner may not have the option to demand compensation for diminution.
The new ruling follows the qiyas because the illa itself has changed.
5
Document the transition
Note the original ruling and the updated ruling, citing the source for the change (e.g., Qadi Ismail's observation).
This demonstrates the dynamic nature of fiqh.

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
... It is based indirectly, so it indicates that it also outweighs istislah (public interest) which is not based on text, but only on temporal benefit that is subject to change with changing times and emerging circumstances and requirements.
2/74 - Among the branches of this principle in Hanafi ijtihad are the following applications: 1. The sale of bees and silkworms is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah because he did not consider them as property, by analogy to other crawling creatures like lizards and frogs. But Imam Muhammad, one of his companions, ruled that they have monetary value and their sale is valid due to the custom of people dealing in them by sale and purchase.
2. The analogical principle requires that the judge listen to every case brought before him, then rule for or against the plaintiff according to what is proven. But the jurists abandoned this analogy in the case where a wife who has been consummated claims that her husband did not pay her any of the prompt dower and demands judgment against him for the entire prompt dower. They said: her claim is not heard; rather the judge rejects it without even asking the husband. They justified this by custom, because the normal practice of people is consistent and rarely deviates that a woman is not given to her husband until he pays part or all of the prompt dower. So her claim is contradicted by the apparent state of affairs after consummation, so it is not heard. (Radd al-Muhtar, chapter of Mahr, 3/363 and Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, beginning of Mahr (1)(20/1)
(1) Note that this custom in our time is no longer consistent in our Syrian lands, though it is still prevalent. Many women are given without receiving any dower to ease the burden on the husband. So this claim should be heard today.
Al-Qarafi in his book al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa 'an al-Ahkam, and the actions of the judge and imam, reported from Malik that if the wife after consummation claims non-receipt of her dower, the word is the husband's, even though the analogical principle is non-receipt.
932

Chapter (74) Conflict of Custom and Ijtihad
3. The analogical rules require that it is not permissible to pay a debt to other than its owner, and its receipt does not bind the creditor unless the receiver has authority from the creditor (guardianship or agency). But the jurists abandoned the analogy in the case of an adult virgin daughter if her father, or her grandfather in the father's absence, received her dower from her husband at her marriage. They considered this receipt effective against her and discharging the husband's liability due to custom and habit, unless she forbade payment to others.
There are many branches where jurists abandoned the ruling of analogy for the conflicting custom.
3/74 - Secondly: On analogy based on a customary cause: If this is the authority of custom in fiqh with respect to analogy based on inherent similarity in the causes between textual rulings and analogized rulings, then it indicates a fortiori the change of analogical rulings with the change of custom when the cause of the analogy itself is based on custom. In that case, change in ruling following the change of custom is not a departure from analogy but rather in line with it.
1. In the Hanafi school, the opinion of its Imam Abu Hanifah was that if a usurper dyes a garment black, that is considered a defect, so the owner has the choice: either leave it with him and claim its value as it was when usurped undyed, or take the garment with compensation from the usurper for the decrease in value due to this dye, according to the general analogical ruling on defects in usurped property with the usurper.
= And after him it is narrated from Qadi Isma'il that this is based on the custom of the people of Madinah in Malik's era that a man does not consummate with his wife until she receives all her dower. He said: Today their customs are contrary to that, so the word becomes the woman's with her oath regarding non-receipt due to differing customs.
(See al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa 'an al-Ahkam, Answer to Question 39, p. 68)
933
Source pages p. 873

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of custom ('urf) in Hanafi fiqh and its interaction with qiyas (analogy). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that custom can override analogical rulings. He gives three examples from Hanafi jurisprudence: (1) Abu Hanifa forbade selling bees and silkworms by analogy to vermin, but Imam Muhammad allowed it due to customary dealing; (2) a wife's claim for unpaid dower after consummation is normally rejected because custom dictates she wouldn't be given without payment, though the footnote notes this custom has changed in modern Syria; (3) a father's receipt of his adult daughter's dower is valid by custom, despite the analogical rule requiring agency. Then he discusses a second principle: when the illa (cause) of a qiyas is itself custom-based, the ruling changes with custom. He gives the example of dyeing usurped cloth: Abu Hanifa considered it a defect, but if custom values the dye job differently, the ruling adjusts. The text is a qawida (maxim) with its furuat (applications), showing how custom operates as a source of law that can modify existing rulings.

Key Terms
العلة العرفية (al-illa al-urfiyya): العلة التي تستند إلى عرف الناس في تحديد شيء كالتعييب
التغييب (al-ta'yib): جعل الشيء معيباً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Qadi Isma'il argue that the ruling on the wife's dower claim should be heard today?
A) Because the wife is always truthful.
B) Because the custom that women are not given without full dower has changed.
C) Because Abu Hanifa's ruling was wrong.
D) Because the husband's claim is weaker.

Key Takeaway
When the illa of a qiyas is custom-based, the ruling changes with the custom, maintaining the integrity of qiyas rather than abandoning it.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate

Customary Circumstantial Evidence (Qarīna 'Urfiyya) and Its Judicial Role
Definition, classification and judicial application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conclusive and preponderant circumstantial evidence (qara'in) and apply the Hanafi qawida that customary qara'in act as initial preponderators in judicial proceedings.

Core Idea
Customary circumstantial evidence shifts the burden until stronger evidence appears.

Circumstantial evidence touches every corner of Islamic jurisprudence. But not all signs are equal: smoke proves fire, but a sheep before Eid only suggests sacrifice. This lesson unpacks how Hanafi jurists classify and weigh circumstantial evidence, especially the customary type (qarīna 'urfiyya), and how they deploy it as an initial preponderator in court. By understanding this, you will see how custom (ʿurf) becomes a legal tool for balancing claims without demanding impossible proof.

Guided Path
1
Define the key term 'qarīna' and its types
Extract the definition from the text: every apparent sign accompanying something hidden and indicating it. Then split the two types: rational (logical connection) and customary (based on custom).
Rational qara'in are fixed; customary qara'in change with society.
2
Identify the evidentiary strength scale
From the text, note that qara'in vary from conclusive (like smoke for fire) to weak (mere probability). The jurists use this scale to decide if a qarina alone suffices for judgment.
Conclusive qara'in are rare; most are probabilistic.
3
State the qawida governing customary qara'in
The passage states that non-conclusive customary qara'in are treated as 'al-zahir' and serve as initial preponderators for the claim of the litigant they support, combined with his oath, until stronger evidence overcomes them.
This qawida is one of the intermediate (wusta) principles of Hanafi legal maxims.
4
Apply the qawida to the furuat (examples)
Work through the spousal dispute over household items. The qarina of customary usage assigns the burden: husband gets men’s items, wife gets women’s items. Then handle the exception (craftsman/merchant) and the ship-with-flour example.
The exception shows that a stronger customary qarina (occupation) can reverse the presumption.
5
Note the connection to other qawaid
Recognise how this qawida relates to 'al-ʿāda muḥakkama' (custom is authoritative) and 'al-yadu ẓāhiratu al-milk' (possession indicates ownership).
These maxims work together in property disputes.

القرائن العرفية
٢/٧٥ - القرائن جمع قرينة والمراد بها كل أمارة ظاهرة تقارن شيئاً خفياً فتدل عليه وهي مأخوذة من المقارنة بمعنى المرافقة والمصاحبة. ودلالة القرائن على مدلولاتها تتفاوت في القوة والضعف تفاوتاً كثيراً: فقد تصل في القوة إلى درجة الدلالة القطعية كالرماد أو الدخان، فإنهما قرينة قاطعة على وجود النار. وقد تضعف حتى تنزل دلالتها إلى مجرد الاحتمال .
والقرينة قد تكون عقلية وقد تكون عرفية :
فالقرينة العقلية هي التي تكون النسبة بينها وبين مدلولها ثابتة يستنتجها العقل دائماً، كوجود المسروقات عند المتهم بالسرقة .
والقرينة العرفية هي التي تكون هذه النسبة بينها وبين مدلولها قائمة على عرف أو عادة تتبعها دلالتها وجوداً ،وعدماً، وتتبدل بتبدلهما، كشراء المسلم شاة قبيل عيد الأضحى، فإنها قرينة على قصد الأضحية، وكشراء الصائغ خاتماً فإنه قرينة على أنه اشتراه للتجارة فلولا عادة التضحية عند الأول، والتجارة بالمصوغات عند الثاني لما كان ذلك قرينة.

١ - إذا اختلف الزوجان في بعض أمتعة البيت أنها ملك الرجل أو المرأة، ولا بينة لأحدهما يترجح قول الرجل بيمينه فيما يستعمله الرجال عادة كالسيف وثياب الرجال فيحكم له به مبدئياً، ويترجح كذلك قول المرأة فيما يستعمله النساء كثيابهن وأدواتهن ؛ وذلك بقرينة عادة الاستعمال وعرفه، وإن كان من المحتمل أن يملك كل منهما ما هو في العادة من حوائج الآخر.
وهذا ما لم يكن أحدهما صانعاً أو تاجراً في النوع الصالح للآخر، فإن كان كذلك فالقول عندئذ له في ملكيته لانعكاس الآية .

The Ninth Chapter: Theory of Custom. Customary Circumstantial Evidence. 2/75 - Al-Qara'in is the plural of qarina, and by it is meant every apparent sign that accompanies something hidden and indicates it, derived from muqarana meaning accompaniment. The indication of qara'in to their significations varies greatly in strength: sometimes it reaches the degree of definitive indication, like ashes or smoke, for they are conclusive evidence of fire. And sometimes it weakens until its indication descends to mere probability. And the qarina may be rational or customary. The rational qarina is that whose relation to its significand is fixed, always deduced by the mind, like the presence of stolen goods with the theft suspect. The customary qarina is that whose relation to its significand is based on a custom or habit, its indication following them in existence and non-existence, and changes with their change. Like a Muslim buying a sheep shortly before Eid al-Adha, it is evidence of the intention of sacrifice; and like a jeweler buying a ring, it is evidence that he bought it for trade. For without the custom of sacrifice for the first and trade in jewelry for the second, it would not be evidence. 3/75 - Degrees of reliance of jurists on both types of qara'in in adjudication: Islamic fiqh has considered qara'in, regardless of their type, among the establishing proofs relied upon in adjudication to varying degrees: a) If the qarina is conclusive, it alone suffices as final evidence for judgment, as if a person is seen coming out of a house disturbed, with a blood-stained knife in his hand, and inside the house a slaughtered person is found writhing, then the one coming out is considered the killer. (Ref: Majalla/1741 and its commentary). b) As for when it is not conclusive but preponderant, then jurists rely on it as initial evidence by which the claim of one of the litigants, along with his oath, is given preponderance until the contrary is established by stronger evidence. Customary qara'in are of this type. They consider them as what they call 'al-zahir' i.e., apparent state of affairs. And the strength of evidence of these non-conclusive circumstantial indications before the court is that they are considered initial preponderators for the claim of the litigant whom they support until the contrary is proven. 4/75 - Examples of jurists' reliance on customary qara'in in judicial solutions: On this basis, jurists have decided many judicial solutions in various incidents, stipulating: 1. If spouses differ over some household items whether they belong to the husband or the wife, and neither has evidence, the husband's statement with his oath is given preponderance for items customarily used by men, like the sword and men's clothing, so he is initially adjudged to have them. Similarly, the wife's statement is given preponderance for items customarily used by women, like their clothes and tools. This is due to the evidence of customary usage and its custom, even though it is possible that each may own what is normally the other's needs. This is as long as neither of them is a craftsman or merchant in the type suitable for the other; if that is the case, then the statement belongs to him regarding ownership due to the reversal of the situation. As for items suitable for both, like furniture and cabinets, the husband's statement is initially given preponderance because he is considered the possessor of them, and possession is another qarina from the apparent indications of ownership. (Ref: Majalla/1771 and its commentaries, and Mu'in al-Hukkam, Part II, Chapter 28 on adjudication by custom and habit, p. 161, and Chapter 50 on adjudication by qara'in, p. 204). 2. And similarly they said regarding a ship loaded with flour if a sailor and a merchant dispute...
Source pages p. 877

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am discusses the theory of custom, specifically focusing on circumstantial evidence (qara'in). The author first defines qarina as an apparent sign that indicates something hidden, derived from the concept of accompaniment. He notes that qara'in vary in strength from conclusive (like smoke indicating fire) to weak (mere probability). He then classifies qara'in into two types: rational (عقلية) and customary (عرفية). Rational qara'in are based on a fixed logical relation, such as stolen goods found with a suspect. Customary qara'in depend on social customs and change accordingly, like buying a sheep before Eid indicating intent to sacrifice, or a jeweler buying a ring for trade. The author then explains how jurists employ qara'in in adjudication. Conclusive qara'in alone suffice for judgment, as in the example of a person emerging from a house with a bloody knife and a slain body inside. Non-conclusive but preponderant qara'in, including customary ones, serve as initial evidence that tilts the balance in favor of one litigant's claim, supported by his oath, until stronger evidence contradicts it. This is called 'al-zahir' (apparent state). The author provides practical examples: in a dispute between spouses over household items, customary usage determines initial ownership (men's items to husband, women's to wife). If one spouse is a craftsman or merchant in the other's typical items, the presumption reverses. For items usable by both, the husband's claim prevails due to possession (yad) as another qarina. The second example begins about a ship loaded with flour when a sailor and a merchant dispute, but the text cuts off. This passage illustrates how customary qara'in are integrated into Hanafi legal procedure as evidentiary tools, not as definitive proof but as initial indicators that align with the 'urf (custom).

Key Terms
قرينة (qarīna): Every apparent sign that accompanies something hidden and indicates it, used as circumstantial evidence.
قرينة عرفية (qarīna 'urfiyya): A qarina whose indication is based on customary practice, changing with custom.
ظاهر (ẓāhir): The apparent state of affairs that serves as initial evidence until proven otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do Hanafi jurists treat customary circumstantial evidence (qarina 'urfiyya) in adjudication?
A) They treat it as conclusive proof by itself.
B) They rely on it as initial evidence that preponderates one litigant's claim with his oath until contrary is proven.
C) They only accept it if supported by two male witnesses.
D) They reject it entirely because it is based on custom.

Key Takeaway
Customary circumstantial evidence (qarīna 'urfiyya) is a tool for initial judicial inclination, not a definitive proof, and its application varies with custom.

Customary Circumstantial Evidence: Concept and Hanafi Application
Explains the evidentiary value of non-definitive customary circumstantial evidence in court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and evidentiary role of customary circumstantial evidence (القرائن العرفية) in Hanafi jurisprudence and apply it to the examples of spousal disputes over household items and ship-and-flour disputes.

Core Idea
Customary circumstantial evidence preponderates one litigant's claim until contradicted by stronger proof.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm explains that jurists treat non-definitive but preponderant circumstantial evidence as a primary proof. These indicators, called 'al-zahir', are combined with an oath to tilt the balance in favor of one party. The text then gives concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh to illustrate how custom governs initial judicial rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept
What are customary circumstantial evidence and what is their probative value? They are non-definitive but preponderant indicators that serve as a primary proof (دليل أولي) in court.
They are called 'al-zahir' (apparent state of affairs).
2
Understand the role of oath
How does the oath complement the evidence? The party favored by custom must also take an oath (يمين) to solidify the initial ruling.
The oath does not prove ownership; it merely confirms the preponderance.
3
Analyze the first example
Why is the husband's statement preponderated for men's items? Customary usage (عادة الاستعمال) indicates that swords and male garments are typically used by men, so the husband's claim is preferred with his oath.
The same reasoning applies to women's items.
4
Analyze the exception
What happens if one spouse is a craftsman or trader in the type suitable for the other? The presumption reverses: the craftsman/trader is favored because of the reversal of the indication (انعكاس الآية).
For items suitable for both (e.g., furnishings), the husband's statement preponderates due to possession (اليد).
5
Analyze the second example
How does custom assign the ship and flour? The flour is initially judged for the merchant, the ship for the boatman, based on customary association.
This is until stronger evidence proves otherwise.

Source Text

الفصل (٧٥) القرائن العرفية
ب - أما إذا كانت غير قطعية الدلالة ولكنها أغلبية، فإن الفقهاء يعتمدونها دليلاً أولياً يترجّح بها زعم أحد المتخاصمين مع يمينه حتى يثبت خلافها ببينة أقوى.
ومن هذا القبيل القرائن العرفية. فهم يعتبرونها من قبيل ما يسمونه «الظاهر» أي ظاهر الحال ودلالة الظواهر غير القطعية هذه هي قوتها الإثباتية أمام القضاء، فهي تعتبر مرجّحات أولية لزعم من تشهد له من الخصمين حتى يثبت خلافها .
٤/٧٥ - أمثلة اعتماد الفقهاء على القرائن العرفية في الحلول القضائية : وعلى هذا الأساس قرّر الفقهاء حلولاً قضائية كثيرة في شتى الحوادث فنصوا على أنه :
١ - إذا اختلف الزوجان في بعض أمتعة البيت أنها ملك الرجل أو المرأة، ولا بينة لأحدهما يترجح قول الرجل بيمينه فيما يستعمله الرجال عادة كالسيف وثياب الرجال فيحكم له به مبدئياً، ويترجح كذلك قول المرأة فيما يستعمله النساء كثيابهن وأدواتهن ؛ وذلك بقرينة عادة الاستعمال وعرفه، وإن كان من المحتمل أن يملك كل منهما ما هو في العادة من حوائج الآخر.
وهذا ما لم يكن أحدهما صانعاً أو تاجراً في النوع الصالح للآخر، فإن كان كذلك فالقول عندئذ له في ملكيته لانعكاس الآية .
أما ما يصلح لكل منهما كالمفروشات والخزائن فيترجح فيه مبدئياً قول الزوج، لأنه يعتبر هو صاحب اليد عليه، واليد قرينة أخرى من دلالات الظاهر على الملك (ر: المجلة / ۱۷۷۱ / وشروحها، ومعين الحكام القسم الثاني الباب //۲۸ في القضاء بالعرف والعادة ص/ ١٦١، والباب /٥٠/ في القضاء بالقرائن ص/ ٢٠٤).
۲ - وكذلك قالوا في سفينة مشحونة دقيقاً إذا اختلف ملاح وتاجر دقيق على ملكيتها بما فيها، ولا بينة لأحدهما، إنه يحكم مبدئياً بالدقيق لتاجره، وبالسفينة للملاح، حتى تقوم بينة على إثبات خلافه .
وهكذا اعتبروا سائر القرائن العرفية المشابهة من المرجحات الأولية التي يعتمدها القضاء اليمين في ترجيح قول أحد الخصمين.
ر المجلة، باب : القول لمن). :
وهذا أيضاً مذهب المالكية نصَّ عليه صاحب «التبصرة» فيما لو اختلف عطار ودباغ في العطر والجلد أو اختلف فقيه وحداد في نحو الجبة والكير إلخ ... تفريعاً على أصل القضاء بالعرف والعادة.
أما الشافعية فلم يقبلوا القضاء بهذه القرائن على ظن أنه يخالف الحديث النبوي القائل : البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» (ر: ف .(٢٣/٥)
والواقع أنه ليس بينهما مخالفة كما أوضحه صاحب «تهذيب الفروق (۱).
لك ٥/٧٥ - يتضح من الأمثلة السالفة أنَّ القرائن العرفية هي دلائل قضائية يرجح معها العقل احتمالاً على آخر ترجيحاً يستند فيه إلى علاقة وصلة يؤيدها الأمر المتعارف المعتاد فمرجعها في النهاية إلى حكم العقل وليست من صلب نظرية العرف الذي يحكم في تحديد الحقوق والالتزامات وتقييد التصرفات وتفسير العقود، وتخصيص النصوص (۲)
(۱) ر: رسالة العرف والعادة للأستاذ أبي سنة ص/ ۱۱۸ - ۱۱۹/ نقلاً عن تكملة فتح القدير، وتكملة رد المحتار ،والبحر آخر باب التحالف، والزيلعي أول كتاب الدعوى، والتبصرة في الفقه المالكي ،۵۸/۲ ، وتهذيب الفروق المطبوع في هامش فروق القرافي الفرق / ١٦٠ ج ۳ ص ۱۸۵ ، والمهذب للشيرازي في الفقه الشافعي جا ص ٣٣٥.
(۲) وقد قدمنا نقلاً عن علماء الأصول في تعريف العادة أنها: «الأمر المتكرر من غير علاقة عقلية». والعادة أخت العرف (ر: ف ٨/٦٧) .
Chapter (75) Customary Circumstantial Evidence
B - But if the indications are not definitive but are preponderant, the jurists rely on them as a primary proof by which the claim of one of the litigants is preponderated along with his oath, until its contrary is established by stronger evidence.
Of this type are customary circumstantial evidence. They consider them as what they call 'al-zahir' i.e., the apparent state of affairs, and the indication of non-definitive circumstantial evidence is its probative strength before the judiciary, so they are considered primary preponderators for the claim of the one they support among the two litigants until its contrary is established.
4/75 - Examples of jurists' reliance on customary circumstantial evidence in judicial resolutions: On this basis, the jurists decided many judicial resolutions in various incidents. They stipulated that:
1. If the spouses disagree about some household items as to whether they belong to the husband or the wife, and there is no evidence for either, the husband's statement with his oath is preponderated regarding what men usually use, such as swords and men's clothing, so it is initially judged for him. Similarly, the wife's statement is preponderated regarding what women usually use, such as their clothes and tools; this is due to the presumption of custom and usage, even though it is possible that each may own what is ordinarily needed by the other.
This is unless one of them is a craftsman or merchant in the type suitable for the other, for then the statement is for him regarding its ownership due to the reversal of the indication.
As for what is suitable for both, such as furnishings and cabinets, the husband's statement is initially preponderated, because he is considered the possessor of them, and possession is another circumstantial evidence from the apparent indications of ownership (ref: Majallah /1771/ and its commentaries, Mu'in al-Hukkam, section two, chapter 28 on judging by custom and habit p. 161, and chapter 50 on judging by circumstantial evidence p. 204).
2. Similarly, they said regarding a ship loaded with flour if the boatman and a flour merchant dispute over ownership of what is in it, and there is no evidence for either, that the flour is initially judged for the merchant and the ship for the boatman, until evidence is established to prove otherwise.
Thus they considered all similar customary circumstantial evidence as primary preponderators that the judiciary relies on with the oath to preponderate the statement of one of the litigants.
Ref: Majallah, chapter: 'The statement is for the one who...'
And this is also the Maliki school, as stated by the author of al-Tabsirah concerning a perfumer and a tanner disputing over perfume and leather, or a jurist and a blacksmith disputing over a cloak and bellows, etc., as a branching from the principle of judging by custom and habit.
As for the Shafi'is, they did not accept judging by these circumstantial evidence, thinking it contradicts the prophetic hadith: 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the denier' (ref: F. 5/23).
But in reality there is no contradiction between them, as explained by the author of Tahdhib al-Furuq (1).
5/75 - It is clear from the preceding examples that customary circumstantial evidence are judicial indicators by which the mind preponderates one probability over another based on a relationship and connection supported by what is customary and habitual. Ultimately their reference is to the ruling of reason, and they are not part of the theory of custom that governs in determining rights and obligations, restricting dispositions, interpreting contracts, and specifying texts (2).
(1) Ref: Risalah al-Urf wa al-Adah by Prof. Abu Sinnah p. 118-119 quoting from Takmilah Fath al-Qadir, Takmilah Radd al-Muhtar, al-Bahr (last chapter of al-Tahalf), al-Zayla'i (first chapter of al-Da'wa), al-Tabsirah in Maliki fiqh 2/58, Tahdhib al-Furuq printed in margin of Furuq al-Qarafi vol. 3 p. 185, and al-Muhadhdhab by al-Shirazi in Shafi'i fiqh vol. 1 p. 335.
(2) And we have previously quoted from the scholars of usul the definition of habit as: 'The repeated matter without a rational relationship.' And habit is the sister of custom (ref: F. 67/8).
Source pages p. 878

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the role of customary circumstantial evidence (القرائن العرفية) in judicial proceedings. The author classifies such evidence as non-definitive but preponderant (أغلبية), meaning it is not certain but strongly indicative. Jurists treat it as a primary proof (دليل أولي) that, when combined with the claimant's oath, tilts the balance in favor of one litigant until the other produces stronger evidence (بينة أقوى). This is the concept of 'الظاهر' (apparent state of affairs).

The passage then provides two concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh:
1. Dispute between spouses over household items: Items typically used by men (e.g., swords, male garments) are initially assigned to the husband upon his oath; items typically used by women (e.g., female clothes, tools) to the wife upon her oath. This presumption is based on customary usage (عادة الاستعمال). However, if one spouse is a craftsman or trader in the type of goods suitable for the other, the presumption reverses. For items equally suitable for both (e.g., furnishings), the husband's statement is initially preponderated because he is considered the possessor (ذو اليد) — possession itself being another circumstantial evidence.
2. Dispute between a boatman and a flour merchant over a flour-laden ship: The flour is initially assigned to the merchant, the ship to the boatman, based on custom until proven otherwise.

The author also notes that the Maliki school follows the same principle, citing examples from al-Tabsirah. The Shafi'i school, however, rejected such reliance on customary circumstantial evidence, arguing it contradicts the Prophetic hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' Al-Zarqa clarifies that there is no real contradiction, as the customary evidence merely serves as a preponderating factor, not a substitute for the oath.

Finally, the author emphasizes that customary circumstantial evidence are judicial indicators based on rational inference, distinct from the theory of custom (العرف) that defines rights, obligations, and interpretations of contracts and texts. They are practical tools for the judge, not part of the substantive legal norms.

Key Terms
القرائن العرفية (al-qarā'in al-'urfiyyah): Circumstantial evidence derived from common custom or habitual practice, used as a preponderating factor in judicial decisions.
الظاهر (al-zāhir): The apparent state of affairs that is not definitive but serves as a basis for initial judicial rulings.
المرجحات الأولية (al-murajjiḥāt al-awwaliyyah): Primary preponderators: factors that initially tilt the balance in favor of one litigant's claim before definitive evidence is presented.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath taken by the litigant whose claim is preponderated by the circumstantial evidence, to solidify the initial ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi court, a boatman and a flour merchant dispute over a flour-laden ship. According to the passage, what is the initial ruling?
A) The boatman gets the flour and the merchant gets the ship.
B) The merchant gets the flour and the boatman gets the ship.
C) They share everything equally.
D) The judge waits for more evidence.

Key Takeaway
Customary circumstantial evidence serves as a primary preponderator in Hanafi courts, supporting one litigant's claim with his oath until contradicted by stronger evidence.

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Customary Circumstantial Evidence
Examines the inter-madhhab dispute regarding the admissibility of customary circumstantial evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on customary circumstantial evidence, identify the hadith cited by the Shafi'is, and evaluate the Hanafi response that resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Customary evidence does not replace the oath; it merely preponderates, so no contradiction with the hadith.

This passage from al-Zarqa also presents the Shafi'i objection to using customary circumstantial evidence, based on the well-known hadith 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر.' Al-Zarqa then clarifies that the Hanafi position does not contradict this hadith because the oath is still required. Furthermore, he distinguishes customary circumstantial evidence from the broader theory of custom (العرف), emphasizing that the former is a judicial indicator rooted in rational inference, not a substantive legal norm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i objection
What hadith do the Shafi'is cite and why do they think it prohibits using customary circumstantial evidence? The hadith assigns the burden of proof to the claimant and the oath to the denier.
The hadith is متفق عليه: 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر'.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
How do Hanafis argue that there is no contradiction? Customary circumstantial evidence is only a preponderator, not a substitute for the oath; the oath is still taken.
The evidence does not remove the obligation of the oath; it merely determines who takes it.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in court? In a Hanafi court, the judge initially favors the party supported by custom, requiring the opponent to produce stronger evidence. In a Shafi'i court, the judge would require both parties to present evidence without such initial presumption.
The difference is in the initial procedural advantage.
4
Distinguish from substantive custom
How is circumstantial evidence different from the custom that defines legal norms? Customary circumstantial evidence is a judicial indicator based on rational inference (حكم العقل), not a source of law that determines rights, interprets contracts, or specifies texts.
Substantive custom (العرف) is a source of law; circumstantial evidence is merely probative.
5
Recognize the rational basis
Why is this evidence ultimately based on 'hukm al-'aql'? The preponderance is achieved by rational connection between the custom and the claim, not by textual authority.
The evidence is derived from the mind's assessment of probabilities, not from revealed texts.

Chapter (75) Customary Circumstantial Evidence
B - But if the indications are not definitive but are preponderant, the jurists rely on them as a primary proof by which the claim of one of the litigants is preponderated along with his oath, until its contrary is established by stronger evidence.
Of this type are customary circumstantial evidence. They consider them as what they call 'al-zahir' i.e., the apparent state of affairs, and the indication of non-definitive circumstantial evidence is its probative strength before the judiciary, so they are considered primary preponderators for the claim of the one they support among the two litigants until its contrary is established.
4/75 - Examples of jurists' reliance on customary circumstantial evidence in judicial resolutions: On this basis, the jurists decided many judicial resolutions in various incidents. They stipulated that:
1. If the spouses disagree about some household items as to whether they belong to the husband or the wife, and there is no evidence for either, the husband's statement with his oath is preponderated regarding what men usually use, such as swords and men's clothing, so it is initially judged for him. Similarly, the wife's statement is preponderated regarding what women usually use, such as their clothes and tools; this is due to the presumption of custom and usage, even though it is possible that each may own what is ordinarily needed by the other.
This is unless one of them is a craftsman or merchant in the type suitable for the other, for then the statement is for him regarding its ownership due to the reversal of the indication.
As for what is suitable for both, such as furnishings and cabinets, the husband's statement is initially preponderated, because he is considered the possessor of them, and possession is another circumstantial evidence from the apparent indications of ownership (ref: Majallah /1771/ and its commentaries, Mu'in al-Hukkam, section two, chapter 28 on judging by custom and habit p. 161, and chapter 50 on judging by circumstantial evidence p. 204).
2. Similarly, they said regarding a ship loaded with flour if the boatman and a flour merchant dispute over ownership of what is in it, and there is no evidence for either, that the flour is initially judged for the merchant and the ship for the boatman, until evidence is established to prove otherwise.
Thus they considered all similar customary circumstantial evidence as primary preponderators that the judiciary relies on with the oath to preponderate the statement of one of the litigants.
Ref: Majallah, chapter: 'The statement is for the one who...'
And this is also the Maliki school, as stated by the author of al-Tabsirah concerning a perfumer and a tanner disputing over perfume and leather, or a jurist and a blacksmith disputing over a cloak and bellows, etc., as a branching from the principle of judging by custom and habit.
As for the Shafi'is, they did not accept judging by these circumstantial evidence, thinking it contradicts the prophetic hadith: 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the denier' (ref: F. 5/23).
But in reality there is no contradiction between them, as explained by the author of Tahdhib al-Furuq (1).
5/75 - It is clear from the preceding examples that customary circumstantial evidence are judicial indicators by which the mind preponderates one probability over another based on a relationship and connection supported by what is customary and habitual. Ultimately their reference is to the ruling of reason, and they are not part of the theory of custom that governs in determining rights and obligations, restricting dispositions, interpreting contracts, and specifying texts (2).
(1) Ref: Risalah al-Urf wa al-Adah by Prof. Abu Sinnah p. 118-119 quoting from Takmilah Fath al-Qadir, Takmilah Radd al-Muhtar, al-Bahr (last chapter of al-Tahalf), al-Zayla'i (first chapter of al-Da'wa), al-Tabsirah in Maliki fiqh 2/58, Tahdhib al-Furuq printed in margin of Furuq al-Qarafi vol. 3 p. 185, and al-Muhadhdhab by al-Shirazi in Shafi'i fiqh vol. 1 p. 335.
(2) And we have previously quoted from the scholars of usul the definition of habit as: 'The repeated matter without a rational relationship.' And habit is the sister of custom (ref: F. 67/8).
Source pages p. 878

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the role of customary circumstantial evidence (القرائن العرفية) in judicial proceedings. The author classifies such evidence as non-definitive but preponderant (أغلبية), meaning it is not certain but strongly indicative. Jurists treat it as a primary proof (دليل أولي) that, when combined with the claimant's oath, tilts the balance in favor of one litigant until the other produces stronger evidence (بينة أقوى). This is the concept of 'الظاهر' (apparent state of affairs).

The passage then provides two concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh:
1. Dispute between spouses over household items: Items typically used by men (e.g., swords, male garments) are initially assigned to the husband upon his oath; items typically used by women (e.g., female clothes, tools) to the wife upon her oath. This presumption is based on customary usage (عادة الاستعمال). However, if one spouse is a craftsman or trader in the type of goods suitable for the other, the presumption reverses. For items equally suitable for both (e.g., furnishings), the husband's statement is initially preponderated because he is considered the possessor (ذو اليد) — possession itself being another circumstantial evidence.
2. Dispute between a boatman and a flour merchant over a flour-laden ship: The flour is initially assigned to the merchant, the ship to the boatman, based on custom until proven otherwise.

The author also notes that the Maliki school follows the same principle, citing examples from al-Tabsirah. The Shafi'i school, however, rejected such reliance on customary circumstantial evidence, arguing it contradicts the Prophetic hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' Al-Zarqa clarifies that there is no real contradiction, as the customary evidence merely serves as a preponderating factor, not a substitute for the oath.

Finally, the author emphasizes that customary circumstantial evidence are judicial indicators based on rational inference, distinct from the theory of custom (العرف) that defines rights, obligations, and interpretations of contracts and texts. They are practical tools for the judge, not part of the substantive legal norms.

Key Terms
القرائن العرفية (al-qarā'in al-'urfiyyah): Circumstantial evidence derived from common custom or habitual practice, used as a preponderating factor in judicial decisions.
الظاهر (al-zāhir): The apparent state of affairs that is not definitive but serves as a basis for initial judicial rulings.
المرجحات الأولية (al-murajjiḥāt al-awwaliyyah): Primary preponderators: factors that initially tilt the balance in favor of one litigant's claim before definitive evidence is presented.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath taken by the litigant whose claim is preponderated by the circumstantial evidence, to solidify the initial ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding customary circumstantial evidence?
A) Hanafis accept it, Shafi'is reject it completely.
B) Hanafis require an oath, Shafi'is do not.
C) Hanafis use it as a preponderator with oath; Shafi'is require evidence from both parties without initial presumption.
D) Hanafis use it only for men's items, Shafi'is for all items.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi acceptance of customary circumstantial evidence as a preponderator does not conflict with the hadith; it is a practical tool grounded in rational inference and custom.

Custom and New Means in Real Estate Contracts
Explaining how custom and new regulations change the application of classical rules in real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'La abatha fi al-Shari'ah' to determine when a classical requirement becomes obsolete due to new effective means.

Core Idea
A requirement that becomes futile due to new, more effective means is no longer required — Sharia does not mandate frivolity.

Classical fiqh rules are not static; they respond to changing customs and means. This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm illustrates how the maxim 'There is no frivolity in Sharia' allows classical requirements to be set aside when new institutions achieve their purpose more effectively. We will examine two specific cases in real estate contracts where land registries have replaced old requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing maxim
Locate the maxim 'La abatha fi al-Shari'ah' (لا عبث في الشريعة) in the passage. Understand that this sub-maxim (dabt) prevents insisting on requirements that serve no useful purpose.
The maxim is a branch of the larger principle of custom (العادة محكمة).
2
Analyze the first fur'u: boundary mention
The classical requirement to mention property boundaries aimed at identifying the subject matter. With land registries assigning a unique number, this purpose is fulfilled more precisely. Thus, insisting on boundaries becomes 'abath (futile).
The new means (registry number) provides easier and more complete identification.
3
Analyze the second fur'u: physical delivery
The classical requirement of actual delivery (taslim fi'li) ensured the buyer's control and risk transfer. Registration in the land registry transfers legal ownership and control more effectively than physical handover; hence, registration alone suffices.
Risk of destruction transfers from seller to buyer upon registration, not physical delivery.
4
Extract the underlying reasoning
The reasoning is that the effective cause (illa) behind the old requirements is to ensure certainty of identification and control. When a new means achieves this with greater certainty, the old means become obsolete. The maxim prevents futility.
This is a rational (aqli) argument based on the purposes of Sharia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف وتعطي كلاً منها رقماً خاصاً، كان التعاقد على العقار الغائب عن مجلس العقد لا بد لصحته من ذكر حدود العقار، أي ما يلاصقه من الجهات الأربع، ليتميز العقار المعقود عليه عن غيره وفقاً لما تقضي به القواعد العامة من معلومية محل العقد. ولكن بعد إنشاء السجلات العقارية في كثير من الممالك والبلدان اليوم أصبح يكتفى قانوناً في العقود بذكر رقم محضر العقار دون ذكر حدوده . وهذا ما يوجبه فقه الشريعة، لأنَّ الأوضاع والتنظيمات الزمنية أوجدت وسيلة جديدة أسهل وأتم تعييناً وتمييزاً للعقار من ذكر الحدود في العقود العقارية. فأصبح اشتراط ذكر الحدود عبثاً، وقد قدمنا أنه لا عبث في الشريعة ( ر : ف (٣/٧٦ . . ۲ - وكذلك كان تسليم العقار المبيع إلى المشتري لا يتم إلا بتفريغ العقار وتسليمه فعلاً إلى المشتري، أو تمكينه منه بتسليم مفتاحه ونحو ذلك. فإذا لم يتم هذا التسليم يبقى العقار معتبراً في يد البائع، فيكون هلاكه على ضمانه هو ومسؤوليته وفقاً للأحكام الفقهية العامة في ضمان المبيع قبل التسليم. ولكن بعد وجود الأحكام القانونية التي تخضع العقود العقارية للتسجيل في السجل العقاري استقرَّ الاجتهاد القضائي أخيراً لدينا على اعتبار التسليم حاصلاً بمجرد تسجيل العقد في السجل العقاري. فمن تاريخ التسجيل ينتقل ضمان هلاك المبيع من عُهدة البائع إلى عهدة المشتري لأن تسجيل البيع فيه تمكين للمشتري أكثر مما في التسليم الفعلي، إذ العبرة في الملكية العقارية قانوناً القيود السجل العقاري لا للأيدي والتصرفات. وبتسجيل البيع لم يبق البائع متمكناً أن يتصرّف في العقار المبيع بعقد آخر استناداً إلى وجوده في يده وجميع الحقوق والدعاوى المتفرعة عن الملكية كطلب نزع اليد وطلب الأجرة وغير ذلك، تنتقل إلى المشتري بمجرد التسجيل.
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom. And giving each of them a special number, contracting on real estate absent from the contract session required for its validity mentioning the boundaries of the property, i.e., what adjoins it from the four sides, to distinguish the contracted property from others according to what the general rules require from the knowledge of the subject of the contract. But after the establishment of land registries in many kingdoms and countries today, the law suffices in contracts with mentioning the property record number without mentioning its boundaries. And this is what fiqh of Sharia requires, because temporal conditions and regulations have created a new means easier and more complete in identifying and distinguishing the property than mentioning boundaries in real estate contracts. So stipulating mentioning boundaries became frivolity, and we have previously stated that there is no frivolity in Sharia (ref: vol 3 p76... 2 - And likewise, delivery of the sold property to the buyer was not completed except by vacating the property and actually delivering it to the buyer, or enabling him by handing over the key and the like. If this delivery is not done, the property remains considered in the hand of the seller, so its destruction is on his guarantee and responsibility according to the general fiqh rulings on guarantee of the sold item before delivery. But after the existence of legal rulings that subject real estate contracts to registration in the land registry, judicial ijtihad has finally settled with us to consider delivery occurring merely by registering the contract in the land registry. So from the date of registration, the guarantee of destruction of the sold item transfers from the seller's responsibility to the buyer's responsibility, because the sale registration gives the buyer more enablement than actual delivery, since the criterion in real estate ownership legally is the entries of the land registry, not physical possession and dispositions. And by registration of the sale, the seller is no longer able to dispose of the sold property by another contract based on its being in his hand, and all rights and claims arising from ownership such as eviction claim, rent claim, etc., transfer to the buyer by mere registration.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the impact of custom and new regulations on classical fiqh rules. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, applies the maxim that custom is authoritative and that rulings change with changing times. He gives two examples from real estate contracts. First, the classical requirement to mention the boundaries of a property in a sale contract (to ensure the subject matter is known) is now obsolete because land registries provide a more precise means of identification via a unique number. Second, the classical rule that delivery of a sold property requires physical handover or key delivery is now deemed satisfied by registration in the land registry, because registration provides even stronger legal protection and enablement to the buyer. The author argues that these changes are consistent with Sharia because they follow the principle that there is no frivolity in Sharia—stipulating outdated requirements would be frivolous. The passage uses rational reasoning (dalil aqli) based on the purposes of the law.

Key Terms
السجل العقاري (al-sijjil al-'aqari): Land registry: an official record of real estate ownership and encumbrances
التسليم الفعلي (al-taslim al-fi'li): Actual physical delivery or enabling possession of the sold property
عبث (abath): Frivolity, futility, something without benefit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is mentioning boundaries no longer required in real estate contracts?
A) Because boundaries are not important
B) Because land registries provide a more precise and complete identification via a number
C) Because it was never required in classical fiqh
D) Because the buyer can inspect the property

Key Takeaway
Classical requirements that have become futile due to new effective means are no longer required in Hanafi fiqh, based on the maxim that there is no frivolity in Sharia.

Postponing 'Iddah Start Until Final Verdict: Application of Masalih Mursalah
Explaining that the 'iddah of a judicially divorced woman begins only after the verdict becomes final.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of al-masalih al-mursalah to modify the start of 'iddah in judicial divorces within modern appeal systems.

Core Idea
A judge's divorce verdict triggers 'iddah only when final; masalih mursalah allows this procedural adjustment.

Classical Hanafi law held that a woman's 'iddah begins immediately upon a judge's divorce verdict. However, modern judicial systems allow appeals, meaning the verdict is not final or enforceable until after appeal periods expire. This passage shows how the principle of masalih mursalah (unrestricted public interest) justifies delaying the start of 'iddah until the verdict is final, preserving legal certainty and preventing premature remarriage risks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classical rule
Recall that in classical Hanafi law, a woman's 'iddah for a judicial divorce begins immediately when the judge issues the verdict, because the verdict was final and not subject to appeal.
2
Recognize the new circumstance
Note that modern judicial systems allow appeals (cassation or appeal), so a verdict is not final until appeal deadlines expire or the appeal is rejected. This creates a gap between the verdict and its enforceability.
The change is procedural, not a change in the underlying 'iddah obligation.
3
Apply the principle of masalih mursalah
Use the principle that public interest (masalih mursalah) permits legal adjustments to achieve beneficial outcomes, as long as no definitive text is violated. Conclude that the 'iddah should begin only when the verdict becomes final, to avoid confusion and protect the woman's interests.
This application shows the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh to adapt to new legal systems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter (78): Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to the Evolution of Means. So based on that, it becomes necessary in the fiqh of Sharia that the registration of the real estate contract be considered in the same status as the actual delivery of the property under these new legal regulatory conditions that recognize it, and it is (1). 3 - Islamic law obligated upon every wife who is divorced by her husband an 'iddah (waiting period) to stay a certain period during which her marriage to another man is prevented. This is for Sharia purposes considered part of public order in Islam, the most important of which is ascertaining the emptiness of her womb from pregnancy to prevent mixing of lineages. And in cases where the judge ruled for compulsory divorce or annulment of marriage, the woman was considered to have entered the 'iddah and her 'iddah calculation started immediately from the judge's ruling of separation, because the judge's ruling in the past was issued as final and immediately enforceable, because the judiciary is founded in Sharia on a single level, and there is no one above the judge who has the right to review his ruling. But today our judicial system has made the judge's ruling subject to appeal by way of cassation or by way of appeal or both. And this new judicial organization does not contradict Sharia because it is from matters of public interest subject to the principle of 'masalih mursalah' (unrestricted public interest) (ref: vol 7/5 and after). So if the judge today rules for separation between the spouses, it is necessary that the woman does not enter the 'iddah until after his ruling becomes final, not subject to any means of judicial challenge, either by expiry of the legal deadlines without challenge from the opponent, or by confirmation of the appealed ruling by the appellate court and its rejection of the appeal when it sees the ruling in accordance with principles. Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses how modern judicial systems affect the application of classical fiqh rules. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, illustrates this with the rule of 'iddah for a judicially divorced woman. In classical Hanafi law, the 'iddah of a woman divorced by court order began immediately upon the judge's verdict because the verdict was final and not subject to appeal. However, in modern legal systems that allow appeals (cassation or appeal), the woman should not enter the 'iddah until the verdict becomes final and non-appealable. This is because the ruling is not yet enforceable until it is final. The author justifies this change by the principle of 'masalih mursalah' (public interest), which allows legal adjustments to achieve beneficial outcomes as long as they do not contradict definitive texts. This demonstrates the flexibility of Islamic law to accommodate new circumstances while preserving its objectives.

Key Terms
العدة (al-'iddah): A waiting period after divorce or death during which a woman cannot remarry, to ensure no pregnancy from the former husband
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursalah): Considerations of public interest not specifically mentioned in the primary sources, used as a basis for legislation when not conflicting with textual evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The start of 'iddah in judicial divorce now depends on the finality of the verdict, justified by the principle of masalih mursalah.

Classifying General Legal Maxims: Basic vs. Subsidiary
Methodological classification of qawaid into basic and subsidiary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given Hanafi legal maxim as either basic or subsidiary and explain the relationship between them.

Core Idea
Basic maxims are independent principles; subsidiary maxims are derived from them.

The Majalla contains many general maxims, but not all are equally fundamental. Some are independent principles, while others are derived from broader ones. This lesson teaches you to distinguish between basic and subsidiary maxims, a skill essential for understanding the hierarchy of Hanafi legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the author's definition: basic maxims (al-qawa'id al-asasiyya) are independent and not derived; subsidiary maxims (al-qawa'id al-mutafarri'a) are derived from the basics.
Think of basic maxims as parent rules and subsidiary maxims as child rules.
2
Explain why classification is needed
The author notes that some maxims overlap or are redundant, so systematic classification brings order.
Without classification, similar maxims are scattered, making study difficult.
3
Work through the example
The four maxims on textual interpretation (Articles 60, 12, 61, 62) together form a system. Identify which might be basic and which subsidiary.
For instance, 'The default in speech is literal meaning' is a basic principle, while 'If literal meaning is impossible, recourse is made to metaphor' is subsidiary to it.
4
Analyze the Majalla's arrangement
Critique the Majalla's listing as unsystematic, causing related maxims to be distant. Understand how your own classification would reorganize them.
This shows the importance of a logical order for learning.
5
Recognize the benefit
Studying classified maxims develops quick juristic aptitude and deep understanding of fiqh foundations.
This skill helps in applying maxims correctly in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي والثمانون
عرض القواعد التي جاءت بها المجلة مرتبةً، ومصنفة إلى أساسية وفرعية
مع شرح لها موجز جداً
۱/۸۱ - إنَّ قواعد المجلة التي هي هدف بحثنا في هذا القسم الأخير من الكتاب كلها قواعد كلية ذات صياغة تشريعية فنية بالمعنى السالف البيان .
غير أنها قد جاء في بعض منها شيء من الترادف أو التداخل مع غيرها. ومن ثم وجب تصنيفها إلى صنفين :
١ - القواعد الأساسية التي كل منها أصل مستقل ليس متفرّعاً من قاعدة أعم منه .
٢- القواعد المتفرعة من تلك القواعد الأساسية.
وإن لجنة المجلة لم تصنف هذه القواعد، ولم تراع التناسب والتناسق في عرضها، بل سَرَدتها سرداً غير مرتب تفرّقت وتباعدت فيه القواعد المتقاربة أو المتداخلة في المعنى والموضوع. فمثلاً نجد :
-
في المادة / ٦٠ قاعدة : إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله».
وفي المادة / ۱۲ / من المجلة قاعدة: الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة». وفي المادة / ٦١ / قاعدة: إذا تعذرت الحقيقة يُصار إلى المجاز».

وفي المادة / ٦٢ قاعدة: «إذا تعذر إعمال الكلام يُهمل».
من سلسلة موضوع واحد، أنَّ هذه القواعد الأربع هي حلقات ترسم بمجموعها، وبهذا الترتيب الذي أوردناها به الآن، الدستور الأصولي المرتب في فهم النصوص، والطريقة العلمية الصحيحة في تفسيرها وكيفية العمل بها .
۲/۸۱ - هذا، وبما أنَّ هذه القواعد الكلية قد أدخلت في صلب المجلة وجعلت جزءاً منها عني جميع شرّاح المجلة بشرحها شروحاً متفاوتة في الإسهاب والاقتضاب تبين ما يتفرع عليها من أحكام فقهية، وما يستثنى منها؛ كما أفردت أيضاً بشروح خاصة بها دون سائر المجلة، فإن دراسة هذه القواعد الكلية وإيضاحها مما يعطي الدارس مَلَكَةً فقهية عاجلة، ويلقي نوراً كاشفاً على آفاق الفقه الإسلامي، ومبانيه، فيمشي الطالب في فهمه بخطوات ثابتة مكينة، وبصيرة مُدْرِكة.
/۳/۸/۱ ـ وقد كان من برنامج هدفي في هذا المدخل أن أجعل هذا القسم الثالث الأخير منه شرحاً واسعاً لهذه القواعد التي صدرت بها المجلة غير أني لحظت عند وصولي إلى هذا القسم الأخير أن هذا المدخل قد طال فيه القسمان الأولان، وأن هذه القواعد مشروحة شرحاً كافياً في معظم الشروح المتداولة للمجلة، وإن كان أسلوبها غير الأسلوب التعليمي الذي التزمنا السير عليه ١
(۱) وأوسع وأجل شرح لقواعد المجلة هذه فيما رأيت هو شرح والدي الشيخ أحمد بن الشيخ محمد الزرقا رحمهما الله تعالى. فقد شَرَح هذه القواعد بمناسبة تدريسه إياها لنا في المدرسة الثانوية الشرعية بحلب، ثم استمر يوسع شرحها ويضيف إليه كل ما يجد له صلة بإحدى هذه القواعد من فروع الأحكام والقيود والمستثنيات في مطالعاته الفقهية المستمرة وتدريساته خلال عشرين عاماً، فكان أحفل الشروح بالفوائد والشواهد، وأجمعها للشوارد بحيث يأخذ دارِسُه بأطراف الفقه. وقد أعجلته المنية عن طبعه فبقي مخطوطاً عندي. ثم يسر الله تعالى لي طبعه قبل بضع سنين بعنوان (شرح القواعد الفقهية ثم أعدنا طبعة ثانية في دار القلم: دمشق، ١٤٠٩ هـ / ۱۹۸۹ م
Chapter Eighty-One: Presentation of the maxims brought by the Majalla, arranged and classified into basic and subsidiary, with a very brief explanation. 1/81: The maxims of the Majalla, which are the goal of our research in this final section of the book, are all general maxims with a technical legislative formulation in the sense explained previously. However, some of them involve redundancy or overlap with others. Hence it became necessary to classify them into two types: 1. The basic maxims, each of which is an independent principle not derived from a broader maxim. 2. The subsidiary maxims derived from those basic maxims. The committee of the Majalla did not classify these maxims, nor did it observe proportion and consistency in presenting them; rather, it listed them in an unordered sequence, causing similar or overlapping maxims in meaning and subject to become dispersed and distant. For example, we find: In Article 60 the maxim: 'Giving effect to speech is preferable to neglecting it.' And in Article 12 of the Majalla the maxim: 'The default in speech is literal meaning.' And in Article 61 the maxim: 'If literal meaning is impossible, recourse is made to metaphor.' [PAGE 913] Chapter Ten: The General Maxims in Fiqh. And in Article 62 the maxim: 'If giving effect to speech is impossible, it is neglected.' [These four maxims] are from a single subject series, that these four maxims are links that together, and in this order we have now presented them, outline the ordered usuli constitution for understanding texts, and the correct scientific method for interpreting them and how to apply them. 2/81: Moreover, since these general maxims have been incorporated into the body of the Majalla and made part of it, all commentators of the Majalla have concerned themselves with explaining them in commentaries of varying length and conciseness, showing what jurisprudential rulings branch off from them and what exceptions are made; they have also been treated in separate commentaries apart from the rest of the Majalla. Therefore, studying and clarifying these general maxims gives the student an immediate juristic aptitude and casts revealing light on the horizons of Islamic jurisprudence and its foundations, so that the student proceeds in his understanding with firm, steady steps and perceptive insight. 3/81: It was part of my intended program in this Madkhal to make this third and final section a broad explanation of these maxims that preceded the Majalla, but I noticed upon reaching this final section that this Madkhal had become lengthy in the first two sections, and that these maxims are sufficiently explained in most of the available commentaries on the Majalla, although their style is not the educational style we have committed to following. (1) The most extensive and excellent explanation of these maxims of the Majalla, in my view, is the explanation of my father, Sheikh Ahmad bin Sheikh Muhammad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on them both). He explained these maxims on the occasion of teaching them to us in the secondary shari'ah school in Aleppo, then he continued to expand his explanation and add to it everything he found related to any of these maxims from rulings, restrictions, and exceptions in his continuous jurisprudential readings and teachings over twenty years. It became the richest of commentaries in benefits and evidence, and the most comprehensive of scattered points, such that its student grasps the entirety of fiqh. Death hastened him before its printing, so it remained in manuscript with me. Then Allah facilitated its printing for me a few years ago under the title 'Sharh al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah', then we reprinted it in a second edition in Dar al-Qalam, Damascus, 1409 AH / 1989 CE.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage opens the section on general maxims (qawaid kulliyya) and discusses their classification. The author explains that the maxims of the Majalla are all general principles, but some overlap or are redundant. Therefore, he categorizes them into two groups: basic maxims (usul independent) and subsidiary maxims (furu' derived from them). He criticizes the Majalla's committee for not logically arranging these maxims, leading to dispersion of related rules. As an example, he points to four maxims about textual interpretation (Articles 60, 12, 61, 62) which together form a systematic usuli method. He then notes that these maxims have been widely commented upon, and studying them quickly develops juristic skill. Finally, he explains his original plan to write a broad commentary, but due to length, he references his father's excellent commentary as already fulfilling that need. This passage is methodological, showing how the author will present the maxims in a structured, educational manner.

Key Terms
القواعد الأساسية (al-qawāʿid al-asāsiyya): The basic maxims, each of which is an independent principle not derived from a broader maxim.
القواعد المتفرعة (al-qawāʿid al-mutafarriʿa): Subsidiary maxims derived from the basic maxims.
المجلة (al-majalla): The Ottoman Majalla (Mecelle), a codification of Hanafi fiqh in the form of legal maxims and articles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Mustafa al-Zarqa, why must the general maxims of the Majalla be classified into basic and subsidiary?
A) Because the Majalla explicitly requires such classification.
B) Because some maxims overlap or are redundant, and systematic organization is needed.
C) Because the Hanafi school only recognizes basic maxims.
D) Because the author wanted to increase the number of chapters.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi legal theory, general maxims are classified into basic (independent) and subsidiary (derived) to resolve overlaps and provide a systematic framework for understanding fiqh.

Three Derivative Maxims from Certainty Not Removed by Doubt
Presents three subordinate maxims derived from the second major maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three derivative maxims from the second major maxim, apply each to a given scenario, and distinguish between the presumption of continuity (istishab) and the presumption of non-existence.

Core Idea
Prior certainties persist, time-established facts remain, and incidental matters are absent until proven otherwise.

The great maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' yields three sub-maxims that form the backbone of evidence law in the Hanafi school. This lesson unpacks each, showing how they govern debts, contracts, and everyday rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context: certainty vs absence
Determine whether there is a prior certainty or not. If a state was certainly true before, istishab applies. If there is no prior certainty, the third maxim applies.
This distinction is critical for choosing the correct maxim.
2
Work through the first derivative (istishab)
Apply the maxim 'The original state remains' to the debt examples. The lender's word is taken because the debt was certainly owed; this presumption continues.
The borrower must prove payment to rebut the presumption.
3
Work through the second derivative (time-established)
Note that this maxim is a restatement of the first, framed temporally. It is used when a state has been established over time.
In practice, it is identical to istishab.
4
Work through the third derivative (original non-existence)
Apply 'The original state in incidental matters is non-existence' to contract conditions. The claimant must prove any additional term.
This maxim applies when there is no prior certainty about the incidental matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد :
۷/۸۱ - أ - الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان (م/ ٥).
-
هذا الأصل يسمى الاستصحاب، وهو اعتبار الحالة الثابتة في وقت ما مستمرة في سائر الأوقات حتى يثبت انقطاعها أو تبدلها.
فلو ادعى المقترض دفع الدين إلى المقرض، أو ادعى المشتري دفع الثمن إلى البائع أو ادعى المستأجر دفع الأجرة إلى المؤجر، وأنكر المقرض أو البائع أو المؤجر، كان القول لهؤلاء المنكرين مع اليمين. أي أن هذه الديون تعتبر باقية في ذمم الملتزمين بها ما لم يثبتوا الدفع، لأنها كانت مستحقة عليهم بيقين فالأصل بقاؤها في ذممهم حتى يثبت سقوطها، وإنما لهم تحليف الدائنين اليمين على عدم القبض فإذا حلفوا قضي لهم. ۸/۸۱ - ب - ما ثبت بزمان يُحكم ببقائه ما لم يوجد دليل على خلافه (م/ ۱۰)
واحدة.

۹/۸۱ - ج - الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم» (م/۹).
المراد بالأمور العارضة ما كان عدمه هو الحالة الأصلية أو الغالبة، فيكون العدم هو المتيقن لأنه الحالة الطبيعية ويكون تغيره إلى الوجود عارضاً مشكوكاً فيه.
۹۸۲
What branches off from it of the maxims: 81/7 - A - The original state of things is presumed to remain (Article 5). This principle is called istishab, and it is considering a state established at a particular time as continuing in all other times until its discontinuation or change is proven. So if the borrower claims payment of the debt to the lender, or the buyer claims payment of the price to the seller, or the lessee claims payment of the rent to the lessor, and the lender, seller, or lessor denies, the word is with these deniers along with their oath. That is, these debts are considered remaining in the obligations of those liable until they prove payment, because they were due from them with certainty, so the original state is their remaining in their obligations until their dropping is proven. And the creditors have the right to demand an oath from them regarding non-receipt; if they swear, judgment is given in their favor. 81/8 - B - What is established by time is judged to remain unless evidence to the contrary exists (Article 10). They are one. This maxim is similar in meaning to the preceding one; all their examples are in practical rulings. 81/9 - C - The original state in incidental matters is non-existence (Article 9). By 'incidental matters' is meant what is originally or predominantly absent, so non-existence is the certain state because it is the natural state, and its change to existence is an incidental matter subject to doubt.
Source pages p. 917

Commentary
This passage presents three derivative maxims that flow from the second major maxim (certainty is not removed by doubt). The first derivative (A) is 'The original state of things is presumed to remain' (Article 5 of the Majalla). This is the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity). It means that if a state is known to have existed at some time, it is presumed to continue until the opposite is proven. The text gives the example of debts: if a borrower claims payment but the lender denies, the lender's word is taken with oath because the original state is that the debt exists. The second derivative (B) is 'What is established by time is judged to remain unless evidence to the contrary exists' (Article 10). This is essentially the same as the first, as the text notes, applied to states established over time. The third derivative (C) is 'The original state in incidental matters is non-existence' (Article 9). This applies when there is no prior certainty: the baseline is that incidental things do not exist. For example, in a contract, the original state is that no condition exists; if someone claims a condition was stipulated, he must prove it. Together, these maxims form the Hanafi approach to presumptions in evidence and continuity.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity: considering a certain state as continuing until the opposite is proven.
الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم (al-asl fi al-umur al-'aridah al-'adam): The original state in incidental matters is non-existence: baseline presumption is that incidental attributes or conditions do not exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim 'The original state of things is presumed to remain,' if a borrower claims repayment and the lender swears an oath denying receipt, what is the effect?
A) The debt is canceled.
B) The debt is confirmed and the borrower must still pay.
C) The debt is suspended pending further evidence.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
The three derivative maxims from 'certainty not removed by doubt' provide clear presumptions for evidence: the known persists, the time-established remains, and the incidental is absent until proven.

The Maxim of Presumption of Innocence of Liability
Explaining the maxim and its evidentiary application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl bara'at al-dhimmah' to determine the burden of proof in a given civil claim scenario.

Core Idea
Every person is born free of liability; the claimant must prove any obligation.

This lesson examines one of the most fundamental legal maxims in Hanafi fiqh: الأَصْلُ بَرَاءَةُ الذِّمَّةِ. This maxim establishes a presumption of innocence of liability, which governs the burden of proof in virtually every civil dispute. Understanding this principle is essential for any mufti or judge assessing claims.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its core meaning
Learn the Arabic text of the maxim: الأَصْلُ بَرَاءَةُ الذِّمَّةِ. It means that the original legal state of a person is freedom from any liability or obligation.
This is a foundational principle in evidence law.
2
Identify the original state (al-asl)
Recognize that every person is born with a dhimmah (liability) that is absolutely clear. Any encumbrance only arises after birth by incidental causes.
'Al-asl' here refers to the presumptive state of non-existence.
3
Understand the burden of proof implication
Since the original state is non-existence of obligation, anyone who claims a deviation from this state must provide evidence. The defendant merely relies on the original state.
The claimant must prove; the defendant's denial is sufficient.
4
Apply to the example: claim of debt
If someone claims a debt against another, the claimant must prove it. The defendant is not required to prove the absence of debt.
This is the furu' (application) given in the text.
5
Generalize to other obligations
The maxim applies to all claims of obligation—whether from contract, tort, or any other cause. The burden of proof always lies with the claimant.
Common misunderstanding: it is not limited to financial claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل براءة الذمة (م/8) لأن المرء يولد خالياً من كل دين أو التزام أو مسؤولية. وكل شغل لذمته بشيء من الحقوق إنما يطرأ بأسباب عارضة بعد الولادة، والأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم، كما رأينا في المادة السالفة. فمن ادعى على غيره التزاماً بدين أو بعمل ما، مهما كان سببه، من عقد أو إتلاف أو أي سبب آخر من أسباب الضمان، فعليه هو الإثبات إذا أنكر الخصم، لأنَّ هذا الخصم يتمسك بحالة أصلية هي براءة الذمة، فيكون ظاهر الحال شاهداً له ما لم يثبت خلافه.
The principle is freedom of liability (Article 8). Because a person is born free from any debt, obligation, or responsibility. Any encumbrance of his liability with any rights only occurs by incidental causes after birth, and the principle regarding incidental matters is non-existence, as we saw in the previous article. So whoever claims upon another an obligation of a debt or an act, regardless of its cause, from a contract, tort, or any other cause of guarantee, it is upon him to prove it if the opponent denies, because this opponent adheres to an original state which is freedom of liability, so the apparent state is a witness for him unless the contrary is proven.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim 'الأصل براءة الذمة' (The Principle of Presumption of Innocence of Liability). It is one of the foundational principles of evidence in Hanafi law. The maxim states that every person is born with a liability free from any obligations. Thus, any claim that a liability exists must be proven by the claimant. The reasoning is that the original state is non-existence of any encumbrance, and the burden of proof is on the one who asserts a departure from this original state. This principle governs the burden of proof in all civil claims. In the example given, if someone claims that another owes a debt, the claimant must prove it; the defendant merely denies and relies on the original state of freedom.

Key Terms
براءة الذمة (bara'at al-dhimmah): The original legal state of a person being free from any liability or obligation.
الأصل (al-asl): The original or default ruling that applies until evidence indicates otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The defendant
B) The claimant
C) Both parties equally
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
The principle الأَصْلُ بَرَاءَةُ الذِّمَّةِ places the burden of proof on the claimant, establishing a presumption of freedom from liability.
