القديم في هذا المقام هو ما لا يوجد وقت التنازع فيه من أدرك مبدأه (۱) (ر: القواعد الفقهية للشيخ محمود حمزة ص/ ١٥٣). والمراد بهذه القاعدة أن ما كان في أيدي الناس أو تحت تصرفاتهم قديماً من أشياء ومنافع ومرافق مشروعة في أصلها يبقى لهم كما قدمه دليلاً على أنه حق قائم بطريق مشروع.

فلو كان لدار إنسان ميزاب على دار غيره أو كان له مسيل أو بالوعة أو ممر في أرض غيره، أو تحميل الجذوع داره على جدار جاره من القديم، فأراد ذلك الغير إزالته فليس له ذلك، بل يحق لصاحبه إبقاؤه ولو لم يعرف بأي وجه وضع، لأنَّ قِدَمه دليل مشروعية وضعه، كما لو كان موضوعاً في الأصل لقاء عوض، أو بطريق القسمة، ونحو ذلك من الأسباب المشروعة. فمنعه ضررٌ لصاحبه لا مسوغ له، إذ لو ساغ ذلك لأدى إلى إهدار معظم الحقوق بعد تقادم عهودها .
أما إذا أحدث الشخص شيئاً من ذلك في ملك غيره إحداثاً معروفاً مبدؤه، فإنه يكلف إثبات سببه ومسوّعه الشرعي، أو يمنع.
وأما إذا كان الشيء القديم ليس من الأمور المشروعة في الأصل فإنه ضرر يزال ولا عبره لقدمه، وهذا موضوع القاعدة التالية :
(۱) بخلاف العيب القديم الذي يظهر في المبيع ويجيز للمشتري فسخ البيع، فإن المراد يقدم العيب هناك أن يكون موجوداً في المبيع وهو في يد البائع قبل تسليمه للمشتري : ولو أنه قد حدث بين العقد والقبض.
Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Jurisprudence. Note: This maxim is conditioned by when the beneficial factor is not greater than the harmful one. Then from it the beneficial factor prevails, for rarely is a matter free from a harm whose absence would be better if possible. And the consideration in legislative command and prohibition is for the predominance of one of two opposites: benefit or harm, good or evil, over the other (ref: F 5/5). Hence it is permissible for a mediator between disputants to lie for reconciliation. And on this basis also the ruling of necessity was built in Sharia, and the maxim 'necessities permit prohibitions' was placed, as will come in 32/81. 28/81 - Y - 'The old is left as it was' (M/6). The old in this context is that whose beginning is not known at the time of dispute. (1) (ref: Al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by Shaykh Mahmud Hamza p. 153). And the meaning of this maxim is that whatever was in people's hands or under their disposal from of old of things, benefits, and facilities that are originally legitimate remains for them, dating back, as evidence that it is a standing right by a legitimate means. And on this basis Abu Yusuf states in Kitab al-Kharaj: 'Nothing is taken from anyone's hand except by a known established right' as previously mentioned (ref: 1/80). So if a person's house has a rainwater spout over another's house, or has a drain or sewer or passage in another's land, or the beams of his house rest on his neighbor's wall from old, and that other wants to remove it, he does not have that right. Rather, its owner has the right to keep it even if it is not known by what means it was placed, because its oldness is evidence of the legitimacy of its placement, as if it was originally placed in exchange for compensation, or by way of partition, and similar legitimate causes. For preventing it is harm to its owner without justification, since if that were permissible, it would lead to the waste of most rights after the lapse of their periods. But if a person creates something of that in another's property by a known beginning, then he is required to prove its cause and legitimate justification, or it is prevented. And if the old thing is not among originally legitimate matters, then it is a harm to be removed and no consideration is given to its oldness, and this is the subject of the following maxim. (1) Contrary to the old defect that appears in the sold item and gives the buyer the right to rescind the sale, for the meaning of 'old defect' there is that it existed in the sold item while in the seller's hand before delivery to the buyer, even if it occurred between the contract and receipt.
Source pages p. 933

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam by Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the first of two interrelated legal maxims from the Majalla: 'The old is left as it was' (Article 6). The author begins with an important qualification: this maxim applies only when the beneficial factor (muqtada) of leaving the old is not outweighed by a greater harm (mani'). In other words, if removing an old arrangement would cause greater harm than keeping it, then it is left; but if the harm from keeping it is greater, the maxim may be overridden. The author gives examples: a mediator may lie for reconciliation (a benefit outweighing the prohibition of lying), and the general principle of necessity (which permits prohibitions). Then the maxim proper is stated: 'al-qadim yutraku 'ala qadamihi'. The term 'al-qadim' is defined as something whose beginning is not known at the time of dispute—thus it is treated as if it were originally legitimate. The passage explains that rights and properties that have been in people's hands for a long time, such as easements (mizab, masil, balu'a, etc.), are presumed legitimate and cannot be removed without proof of illegality. Abu Yusuf's ruling from Kitab al-Kharaj is cited: property cannot be seized without a known right. The examples illustrate: If someone's house has a drainage spout over another's land from time immemorial, the neighbor cannot demand its removal because antiquity implies a legal origin. However, if the installation was recent and known, the owner must prove its legitimacy or it will be removed. A crucial exception: if the old thing is itself originally impermissible (e.g., harmful to the public), it must be removed regardless of age, leading to the next maxim. The footnote clarifies that the 'old defect' in sale law is a different concept: there, an old defect (existing before delivery) gives the buyer a right of rescission, which is not based on this maxim.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadim): ما لا يوجد وقت التنازع فيه من أدرك مبدأه
المقتضي (al-muqtada): العامل الذي يقتضي بقاء الشيء القديم (مثل مصلحة المالك)
المانع (al-mani'): العامل الذي يمنع تطبيق القاعدة (مثل ضرر ناشئ عن البقاء)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
وفقاً للنص، متى يجوز للجار إزالة ميزاب قديم على أرضه؟
أ) إذا كان الميزاب موجوداً منذ أكثر من 30 سنة
ب) إذا كان الميزاب يسبب ضرراً عاماً وغير مشروع في الأصل
ج) إذا كان الميزاب منقولاً
د) لا يجوز أبداً

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-qadim yutraku 'ala qadamihi' is conditional: it only protects the old when the benefit of its preservation is greater than the harm, and it never applies to originally illegitimate matters.

The Restrictive Maxim: 'Harm Does Not Become Old'
Explaining the restrictive maxim that qualifies the previous rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-darar la yakunu qadiman' to determine whether an old structure or practice must be removed, distinguishing between prohibited harm and tolerated old rights.

Core Idea
A prohibited harm is never immunized by antiquity; the old is left only if innocent.

The previous lesson taught that 'the old is left as it is.' But what if the old thing itself is harmful? The second maxim, 'Harm does not become old,' qualifies the first. This lesson unpacks that restriction, showing when antiquity offers no protection and when an exception applies.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its relation
Read the maxim 'al-darar la yakunu qadiman' and note that it restricts the earlier maxim: only non-harmful old things are left as they are.
The maxim does not deny that harm can become old; it denies that age is a valid defense.
2
Identify the key condition
Determine what counts as 'prohibited harm' (darar muharram) – any harm that is originally forbidden in Sharia, such as harming the public or violating privacy.
The harm must be prohibited in its essence, not just any inconvenience.
3
Analyze the furuat
Examine the two applications: (1) a waterspout/sewer on a public road harming the public, (2) a low window overlooking a neighbor's private quarters. Both must be removed despite age.
In both cases, the harm is inherently prohibited (public harm or violation of privacy).
4
Understand the exception
Learn that if the neighbor's house was built after the window existed, the window is not removed because the harm was caused by the newcomer.
This is governed by the original maxim: the old right prevails over the new.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۹/۸۱ - ك - الضرر لا يكون قديماً» (م/٧).
أي لا يحتج بتقادمه، وليس المراد عدم إمكان التقادم فيه.
هذه القاعدة قيد لسابقتها : أي إن المنافع والمرافق التي يحترم قدمها هي التي لا تكون ضرراً ممنوعاً من أصله شرعاً. فإذا كانت كذلك وجبت إزالتها ولا عبرة لقدمها.
-
فلو كان لدار ميزاب أو مجرى أقذار على الطريق مضر بالجماعة يزال مهما تقادم، لأنه غير مشروع في الأصل، إذ الشرع لا يُقر لأحد بوجه من الوجوه حقاً يضر بالعامة.
- وكذا لو كان لإنسان نافذة وطيئة تطل على مقر نساء جاره، فإنها تزال ولو كانت قديمة إلا أن تكون دار الجار هي المحدثة تحت المطل (۱). على أننا قد رأينا تحت القاعدة السابقة أن الممر والمسيل والبالوعة وأمثالها في دار الغير أو أرضه يُحترم قدمها ولو كان وجودها يقيد حرية تصرفه وذلك ضرر له .
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن القوانين العقارية النافذة لدينا منذ عهد الاحتلال الفرنسي قد جاءت بأحكام جديدة في النوافذ والمطلات لم تراع فيها هذه الاعتبارات الشرعية.
29/81 - K - 'Harm does not become old' (M/7). That is, its antiquity cannot be cited as a proof, and it is not meant that antiquity is impossible for it. This maxim is a restriction on its predecessor: that is, the benefits and facilities whose oldness is respected are those that are not a harm prohibited originally by Sharia. If they are thus, then their removal is obligatory and no consideration is given to their oldness. For if a house has a waterspout or a sewage drain on the road harmful to the public, it is removed however old it is, because it is not originally permitted, since Sharia does not recognize for anyone in any way a right that harms the public. Likewise, if a person has a low window overlooking his neighbor's women quarters, it is removed even if old, unless the neighbor's house was built under the window after it existed (1). Then we have seen under the previous maxim that the passage, drain, sewer and the like in another's house or land are respected in their oldness even if their existence restricts his freedom of disposition, and that is harm to him. (1) It is observed in this regard that the real estate laws in force with us since the French occupation have brought new rulings regarding windows and overlooks that do not take into account these Sharia considerations.
Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage presents the second maxim: 'Harm does not become old' (Majalla Article 7). The author clarifies that this is a restriction (qayd) on the previous maxim: the old is left only if it is not originally harmful in Sharia. If the old thing is itself a prohibited harm, its antiquity gives it no immunity. Two clear applications are given: (1) a waterspout or sewer on a public road that harms the public must be removed regardless of how long it has been there, because harming the public is never permitted. (2) Similarly, a window that overlooks a neighbor's private quarters (where women are) must be removed even if it is old, because it violates privacy and is prohibited in Sharia. However, there is an exception: if the neighbor's house was built after the window existed, the window is not removed because the neighbor caused the harm by building there. Then the author notes that the previous maxim's examples (passage, drain, etc. on a neighbor's private property) are respected because they are not originally harmful to the public and they are presumed to have been established legitimately. The footnote observes that modern positive laws in Syria (from the French period) have introduced new rules on windows that do not follow these Sharia principles. This shows how positive law may diverge from fiqh.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-darar): ما كان محظوراً في الأصل شرعاً، كالإضرار بالعامة أو انتهاك الحرمات
النافذة الوطيئة (al-nafidha al-wati'a): الشباك المنخفض الذي يمكن من الاطلاع على ما في الدار المجاورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
وفقاً للنص، ما حكم ميزاب قديم يرسل الأقذار إلى الطريق العام ويضر المارة؟
أ) يبقى لأنه قديم
ب) يزال لأنه ضرر غير مشروع أصلًا
ج) يبقى إذا كان صاحب الدار دفعه لتركيب الميزاب
د) لا يجوز إزالته إلا بحكم قضائي

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar la yakunu qadiman' ensures that prohibited harms cannot acquire legal protection merely by passage of time.

The Criterion for Prescriptive Rights
Defining the criterion for prescriptive rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion to distinguish between long-standing rights that are respected and those that are not, and identify the exception when physical harm is present.

Core Idea
A right is respected by age only if it could have been acquired lawfully; pure harm is never legitimized by time.

In property and nuisance disputes, long-standing use (قدم) is often argued to justify continued interference. This lesson presents a precise legal criterion (ضابط) from Hanafi jurisprudence, derived by Shaykh Ahmad al-Zarqa, that determines when antiquity is respected and when it is not. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti evaluating claims about easements, rights of way, and harmful installations.

Guided Path
1
State the Criterion
Learn the exact wording of the ضابط: 'ما يمكن استحقاقه على الغير بأحد الأسباب المشروعة يُحترم قدمه؛ وإلا فلا.' Understand its two-part structure: first the condition (can be acquired lawfully), then the consequence (antiquity respected or not).
2
Identify the Scope
Recognize that this criterion applies to rights that one person claims over another, such as easements (حق الارتفاق) and private nuisances. It distinguishes between compensable harm (specific to individuals) and non-compensable harm (to the public or morally prohibited).
3
Apply to the Furuat
Work through the three examples in the text: passageway/drain (ممر/مسيل) → antiquity respected; harming public facilities → not respected; contaminated cesspool → the harm must be removed, but the flow of water can be respected if it can be made harmless. For each, link back to the criterion.
4
Identify the Istithna'
Note the exception: even a long-standing harm that pollutes neighbors' wells is not respected because it causes physical damage. This shows that actual harm overrides the respect for antiquity. The governing maxim 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed) takes precedence.
5
Apply in Fatwa Practice
When a mufti faces a case of long-standing use, the first question is: Could this right have been acquired by a legitimate means (contract, gift, etc.)? If yes, and the harm is merely restrictive (not physically damaging), the antiquity is respected. If no (e.g., public harm or physical pollution), the claim fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
فما هو الضابط في التفريق بين ما يحترم قدمه وإن أضرّ بالغير، وبين
ما لا يحترم لقد استنبط والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقا رحمه الله تعالى في شرح
لهاتين القاعدتين ضابطاً تؤيده تعليلات نصوص الفقهاء، خلاصته : أن ما يمكن استحقاقه على الغير بأحد الأسباب المشروعة يُحترم
قدمه ؛ وإلا فلا.
فالممر والمسيل مرتفق يمكن أن يستحق بالتعاقد أو القسمة في الملك المشترك وغير ذلك من الأسباب المشروعة. فيُحترم قدمه عند جهل سببه .. وضرر الخصم منه في تقييد حريته يستحق عليه أمثاله بعقد المعاوضة وغيره لأن كل حق يكتسبه شخص على آخر هو منفعة المكتسبه، ونوع ضرر خاص للآخر، ومع ذلك يستحق عليه بعقد المعاوضة وغيره، ويورث
عن مستحقه .
ففي حالة القدم يمكن حمله على أنه في الأصل وضع بأحد الطرق
المشروعة .
أما إضرار جماعة الناس وإزعاجهم في مرافقهم العامة، فإنه لا يمكن أن يستحق عليهم شرعاً بوجه من الوجوه. وكذا الإطلال على مقرّ نساء الغير، فإنه فسق لا يستحق ولا يقره
الشارع .
ومثل ذلك ما لو كانت بالوعة الدار القديمة تنجس آبار الجيران، فإن قدمها على هذه الصورة لا يحترم كما نص عليه الفقهاء في هذا المقام (إذ لا يجوز شرعاً تنجيس المياه الطاهرة دون مصلحة ولا يستحق ذلك على أحد). ولكن صاحبها يكلف إزالة ضررها بإصلاح مجراها على شكل يضبطها ويمنع تسربها، فيبقى عندئذ مجرَّد مرورها من المنافع التي يمكن استحقاقها على الغير بوجه مشروع فإن لم يمكن إصلاحها وضبطها، أو لم يفعله صاحبها تزال .
Chapter Ten: Universal Legal Maxims. So what is the criterion for distinguishing between that whose antiquity is respected even if it harms others, and that which is not respected? My father, Shaykh Ahmad al-Zarqa, may Allah have mercy on him, derived in his commentary on these two maxims a criterion supported by the reasonings of the jurists. Its summary: that which can be acquired as a right over others through one of the legitimate causes is respected by virtue of its antiquity; otherwise not. So a passageway and a drain are easements that can be acquired by contract or partition in joint ownership and other legitimate causes. Their antiquity is respected when the cause is unknown… And the harm to the opponent from restricting his freedom can be compensated by similar means through a commutative contract etc., because every right a person acquires over another is a benefit for the acquirer and a specific harm to the other, yet it can be acquired through a commutative contract etc., and is inherited from the one entitled. Thus, in the case of antiquity, it can be assumed that it was originally established by one of the legitimate means. As for harming the public and disturbing them in their public facilities, that cannot be acquired as a right over them by any means according to sharia. Likewise, overlooking the private quarters of others' women is immoral, not a right, and the Lawgiver does not approve it. Similarly, if an old house's cesspool contaminates the neighbors' wells, its antiquity in this form is not respected, as the jurists have stated in this context (since it is not legally permissible to contaminate pure water without benefit, nor can such a right be acquired over anyone). Rather, its owner is obliged to remove the harm by repairing its channel in a way that contains it and prevents leakage; then only the mere flow remains as a benefit that can be acquired over others by a legitimate means. If it cannot be repaired and contained, or the owner does not do so, it is removed.
Source pages p. 935

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial criterion (ضابط) for the Hanafi doctrine of prescriptive rights (قدم). The author, adopting his father's insight, explains that the respect given to long-standing arrangements depends on whether the underlying right could have been legitimately acquired. If the benefit (like a right of way or drainage) could have been obtained by contract, division of joint property, or other lawful means, then age is respected even if the exact cause is unknown; the harm to others is considered compensable. However, harm to the public (like blocking a public road) or morally objectionable acts (like overlooking neighbors' houses) can never be legitimized by age because they are not subject to lawful acquisition. The example of a contaminated cesspool shows that nuisance causing actual physical harm (like water pollution) is not excused by antiquity; the owner must remediate it. This criterion is essential for muftis adjudicating disputes over easements and nuisance, balancing property rights with the duty to avoid harm.

Key Terms
الضابط (al-dābiṭ): A criterion or controlling rule that distinguishes between two categories of cases.
القدم (al-qidam): Antiquity or long-standing existence of a right or use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the decisive factor in whether a right of way (ممر) that has been used for a long time is respected?
A) The length of time it has been used.
B) Whether the benefit could have been acquired through a legitimate cause.
C) Whether the owner of the servient land consented initially.
D) Whether it causes harm to the neighbors.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi criterion for prescriptive rights is that antiquity is respected only if the benefit could have been lawfully acquired; otherwise, it is not, especially when actual harm is involved.

The Maxim of Risk and Its Application to Medical Necessity
Establishes a maxim about risk and its application to medical treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-khatar, wa la yajuz al-istirsal, wa mata zala al-khatar 'ada al-hazr' to cases of medical necessity, identifying when a prohibition is temporarily lifted and when it returns.

Core Idea
Risk permits temporary relaxation, but only as long as the risk lasts and only to the minimum needed.

This passage presents a foundational qā‘idah (maxim) in Hanafi medical jurisprudence: when a risk (khatar) is present, a prohibition may be relaxed, but one must not persist in the relaxation after the risk ends. The author applies this to the delicate issue of exposing the 'awrah (private parts) for medical treatment, emphasizing that only the necessary amount may be exposed, and if a female doctor is available, a male doctor must not treat a woman's 'awrah. As a mufti, you will use this maxim to issue precise fatwas that balance necessity and prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its structure
The maxim has two clauses: (a) prohibition of persistence in the relaxation, and (b) return of prohibition when the risk ceases. Commit the Arabic to memory.
2
Identify the first application: treatment of awrah
The rule applies to a doctor exposing the patient's awrah: only the minimum required for treatment is allowed.
3
Identify the second application: gender preference
If a competent female doctor is available, a male doctor must not treat a woman's awrah because same-gender exposure is a lesser prohibition.
4
Connect to the major maxim and its restriction
This qā‘idah is a specific instance of 'al-ḍarūrāt tubīḥ al-maḥẓūrāt' but adds the condition that the permission is temporary and minimal. The related maxim 'mā ubīḥa li-ḍarūratin yuqaddaru bi-qadarihā' reinforces this.
Keep the broader context in mind when issuing fatwas.

الخطر، ولا يجوز الاسترسال ومتى زال الخطر عاد الحظر. فإذا احتيج لمداواة العورة يكشف الطبيب بمقدار ما يحتاج إلى كشفه فقط . وبالنسبة إلى المرأة لا يجوز أن يطلع على عورتها للتطبيب أو التوليد رجل إذا وجدت امرأة تُحسن ذلك، لأنَّ اطلاع الجنس على جنسه أخف محظوراً.
Risk: it is not permissible to persist, and when the risk is removed, the prohibition returns. So if there is a need to treat the private parts (awrah), the doctor may uncover only as much as needed. And as for a woman, it is not permissible for a man to look at her awrah for treatment or delivery if a woman skilled in that exists, because the looking of one gender at the same gender is a lesser prohibition.
Source pages p. 940

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qā‘idah) concerning risk (khaṭar) and its effect on prohibitions. The author states that when a risk or danger is present, one is not allowed to continue in a state of transgression; rather, the prohibition remains until the risk is removed. Once the risk ceases, the original prohibition returns. Two applications are given: (1) For medical treatment of the awrah (private parts), a doctor may expose only the necessary amount – this is permitted due to necessity, but strictly limited. (2) For women, it is not permissible for a male doctor to attend to her awrah for treatment or childbirth if a female doctor is available, because exposure to the same gender is a lesser wrong. This illustrates the principle that necessity (ḍarūrah) permits only what is required, and that the least harmful option should be chosen. The maxim is a specific application of the broader principle that necessities permit prohibitions, but only to the extent needed.

Key Terms
الخطر (al-khaṭar): Danger or risk that justifies relaxation of a prohibition
العورة (al-‘awrah): That part of the body which must be concealed
الاسترسال (al-istirsāl): Continuing or persisting in a state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a male doctor is needed to treat a woman's private parts but a competent female doctor is available?
A) It is permissible for the male doctor to treat her if he covers his eyes.
B) The male doctor is not permitted; the female doctor should be used because same-gender exposure is a lesser prohibition.
C) Both are equally permissible; the patient may choose.
D) The male doctor may treat only with a chaperone present.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'risk permits but only temporarily and minimally' is essential for issuing precise fatwas on medical necessity, ensuring that prohibitions return as soon as the risk ends.

Public Need as Private Necessity: Maxim and Applications
Explaining the maxim with applications and distinguishing need from necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Public need takes the place of private necessity' to identify which exceptions are permanent and general versus temporary and individual, and distinguish it from individual necessity.

Core Idea
A widespread need affecting a community can permanently override general rules, just as necessity temporarily permits the prohibited.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi maxim that bridges necessity and communal need. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains how public need (haja amma) can justify exceptional rulings that are permanent and general, unlike individual necessity which only gives temporary permission. You will see six concrete applications from fiqh and learn the critical distinction between need and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the maxim in Arabic and English, and note that it is a kulliya (universal) wusta (intermediate) maxim governing all fiqh chapters.
The maxim is 'Public need takes the place of private necessity' (الحاجة العامة تنزل منزلة الضرورة الخاصة).
2
Analyze the six furu' (applications)
For each of the six examples (salam, fruit sales, hammam, wafa' sale, changing rulings, custom), explain how the maxim justifies an exception to the general rule.
Focus on the common thread: widespread communal need.
3
Distinguish necessity (darura) from public need (haja amma)
Using the two criteria from the passage: (A) scope of applicability (individual vs. group), and (B) nature of the exception (temporary vs. permanent).
Necessity permits the prohibited for any individual; public need only permits exceptions for a community, and those exceptions are permanent and general.
4
Recognize the correct wording and its implication for fatwa
Note the author's critique of the phrase 'private need' and his preferred wording. Understand that only public need (not individual need) creates permanent rulings.
The mufti must assess whether the need is truly public (affecting a group or profession) before applying the maxim.

الباب العاشر: القواعد الكلية الفقه
حالات الضرورات الملجئَة بل حاجات الجماعة مما دون الضرورة توجب التسهيلات الاستثنائية أيضاً، كما يتضح من التفاريع التالية:
١ - فعلى هذا الأساس شرعت بنصوص الشريعة الأصلية أحكام من قبيل الاستثناء من قواعدها العامة للاحتياج إليها. فقد ورد مثلاً في السنة أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان، ورخص في السَّلم» (ر: ف (٣/٧٢) فترخيص الشريعة في السَّلَم، مع أنه بيع المعدوم الذي منعه النص العام، إنما هو نظر لحاجة كثير من الناس إلى بيع منتوجاتهم واستلاف أثمانها قبل إنتاجها للاستعانة على الإنتاج، ولذا اشترط فيه تعجيل الثمن.
٢ - وعلى هذا الأساس أيضاً جوّز الفقهاء في الأحكام الاجتهادية بيع الثمار التي تتلاحق في الظهور متى بدا بعضها وأمن العاهة، وإن كان معظمها معدوماً لم يبد بعد، نظراً للحاجة.
٣ - وجوزوا أيضاً نزول الحمام بأجر مع جهالة المدة التي سيمكثها الشخص، وجهالة مقدار الماء الذي سيستهلكه، نظراً للحاجة أيضاً.
٤ - وجوزوا الوفاء استثناء من القواعد للحاجة أيضاً (ر: ف ٤٦/ بيع).
٥ - وجميع الأحكام التي قرّر الفقهاء تبدلها لتغير الزمان أو فساده إنما تقرر أحكامها الجديدة وتتبدل تبعاً للحاجة.
٦ - واعتبار العرف عاماً كان أو خاصاً، وتحكيمه في الأحكام إنما هو استجابة لداعي الحاجة، كما تقدَّم بيانه في نظرية العرف.
يتضح من ذلك ومن بقية الأمثلة التي يوردها الفقهاء عن الضرورة والحاجة أنهما يفترقان في الحكم من ناحيتين:
أ - أن الضرورة تبيح المحظور سواءً أكان الاضطرار حاصلاً للفرد أم للجماعة بخلاف الحاجة، فإنها لا توجب التدابير الاستثنائية من الأحكام العامة إلا إذا كانت حاجة الجماعة. وذلك لأن لكل فرد حاجات متجددة ومختلفة عن غيره ولا يمكن أن يقرر لكل فرد تشريع خاص به بخلاف الضرورة فإنها حالة نادرة وقاسرة.
ب - أنَّ الحكم الاستثنائي الذي يتوقف على الضرورة هو إباحة موقتة لمحظور ممنوع بنص الشريعة تنتهي هذه الإباحة بزوال الاضطرار، وتتقيد بالشخص المضطر. أما الأحكام التي تثبت بناء على الحاجة فهي لا تصادم نصاً، ولكنها تخالف القواعد والقياس وهي تثبت بصورة عامة دائمة يستفيد منها المحتاج وغيره.
وبذلك تفترق الحاجة عن العرف الخاص أيضاً، لأن الحكم الذي يثبت بالعرف الخاص يقتصر على أهل العرف فقط كما تقدم.
(من شرح والدي رحمه الله على القواعد بتصرف وإيضاح).
ويلاحظ على صياغة هذه القاعدة أن لفظ الحاجة الخاصة» فيها يوهم لأول وهلة بأنها الحاجة الفردية بينما المقصود كما بينا آنفاً حاجة طائفة من الناس، وليس جميع الناس.
يقول الشيخ العلامة العز بن عبد السلام في كتابه «قواعد الأحكام (١٨٨/٢) (المصلحة العامة كالضرورة الخاصة...).
فالأفضل في صياغة هذه القاعدة والحالة هذه أن تصاغ كما يلي: الحاجة العامة تنزل منزلة الضرورة الخاصة وتفسّر الحاجة العامة حينئذ بأنها ما يشمل جميع الأمة أو طائفة منها كأهل حرفة أو بلد مثلاً.
Chapter Ten: The Major Juristic Maxims. Cases of compelling necessities, and even community needs below the level of necessity, also necessitate exceptional facilitations, as is clear from the following branches: 1. On this basis, the original texts of the Sharia legislated rulings by way of exception from its general rules due to need. For example, it is reported in the Sunnah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling what one does not possess, but permitted salam (advance payment sale). The Sharia's permission of salam, despite it being a sale of a non-existent thing prohibited by the general text, is only due to the need of many people to sell their products and receive their prices in advance before production to assist in production; therefore, prompt payment of the price was stipulated. 2. On this basis also, jurists in ijtihadi rulings permitted the sale of fruits that appear successively once some appear and safety from blight is assured, even though most are non-existent and have not yet appeared, due to need. 3. They also permitted using the public bath for a fee despite ignorance of the duration the person will stay and ignorance of the amount of water he will consume, also due to need. 4. They also permitted wafa' sale as an exception from the rules due to need. 5. All the rulings that jurists have determined to change due to change of time or its corruption are only established and changed according to need. 6. Taking custom into consideration, general or specific, and making it authoritative in rulings is only a response to the call of need, as previously explained in the theory of custom. From this and the remaining examples that jurists cite about necessity (darura) and need (haja), it becomes clear that they differ in ruling in two respects: A. Necessity permits the prohibited whether the compulsion occurs to an individual or to the community, unlike need, which does not necessitate exceptional measures from general rulings unless it is a need of the community. This is because each individual has renewed and different needs, and it is not possible to establish a separate legislation for each individual, unlike necessity, which is a rare and compelling situation. B. The exceptional ruling that depends on necessity is a temporary permission of a prohibited matter prohibited by a text of the Sharia; this permission ends with the end of the compulsion and is restricted to the compelled person. As for rulings established based on need, they do not conflict with a text, but they contradict the rules and analogy, and they are established in a general, permanent manner from which both the needy and others benefit. And by this, need also differs from specific custom, because the ruling established by specific custom is limited to the people of that custom as previously stated. (From my father's commentary on the maxims with modification and clarification). It is noted about the wording of this maxim that the phrase 'private need' suggests at first glance that it is individual need, whereas the intended meaning, as we have explained, is the need of a group of people, not all people. Shaykh al-Allama al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam says in his book Qawa'id al-Ahkam (2/188): 'The public interest is like private necessity...' So the better wording for this maxim in this case is as follows: 'Public need takes the place of private necessity,' and public need is then interpreted as what encompasses the entire nation or a group thereof, such as the people of a certain profession or a certain town.
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on him) is a detailed exposition of the maxim 'Public need takes the place of private necessity' (الحاجة العامة تنزل منزلة الضرورة الخاصة). The author begins by stating that compelling necessities and even community needs below the level of necessity justify exceptional facilitations. He then provides six applications: (1) The Sharia's permission of salam (advance sale) despite the general prohibition of selling non-existent items, due to the widespread need of farmers and traders. (2) Jurists permitting the sale of successive fruits once they begin to appear and are safe from blight, even though most are still non-existent. (3) Permitting the use of public baths (hammam) despite uncertainty in duration and water consumption. (4) Permitting the wafa' sale (a form of security sale) as an exception. (5) Changing rulings due to change of time or moral corruption, based on need. (6) Giving authority to custom, general or specific, as a response to need. The author then distinguishes necessity (darura) from need (haja) in two ways: (A) Necessity permits the prohibited for both individuals and groups, while need only justifies exceptions for a group (public need), because individual needs are too varied to legislate for each. (B) The ruling based on necessity is temporary and ends with the compulsion; the ruling based on need is permanent and general, and it does not contradict a clear text but only departs from analogy. Finally, the author critiques the common wording 'private need' as misleading, quoting al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam that 'public interest is like private necessity.' He recommends the wording: 'Public need takes the place of private necessity,' defining public need as what encompasses the whole community or a substantial group like a profession or a town.

Key Terms
الضرورة (al-darura): A compelling situation that makes a prohibited act permissible to prevent harm to life or essential interests.
الحاجة العامة (al-haja al-amma): A widespread need affecting a significant group of people (e.g., a profession, a town, or the entire umma), which justifies exceptional rulings that are permanent and general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly distinguishes between necessity (darura) and public need (haja amma)?
A) Necessity is temporary and individual; public need is permanent and general.
B) Necessity permits the prohibited for both individuals and groups; public need permits exceptions only for groups.
C) Necessity applies only to matters of life and death; public need applies to any convenience.
D) Necessity is based on textual permission; public need is based on custom only.

Key Takeaway
Public need (haja amma) gives rise to permanent, general exceptions to rules, while private necessity (darura) gives temporary, individual permission.

Refining the Rule of General Need as Equivalent to Necessity
Author analyzes and reformulates the maxim of need versus necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the refined maxim 'General need takes the place of private necessity' to categorize fatwa scenarios and justify rulings based on public need.

Core Idea
General need creates permanent exceptions, individual necessity allows only temporary permissions.

This lesson explores the critical distinction between necessity (darura) and general need (haja 'amma) in Hanafi legal theory. The passage critiques an earlier formulation that used the misleading term 'special need' (haja khassa) and proposes a refined maxim that more accurately captures the scope of need-based exceptions. You will learn how this maxim functions within the system of qawaid and its practical application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original formulation and its problem
Locate the original phrase 'special need' (al-haja al-khassa) and understand why the author finds it misleading—it suggests individual need, while the intended meaning is the need of a group.
The original wording from the Majalla: 'الحاجة الخاصة تنزل منزلة الضرورة'
2
Distinguish necessity (darura) from need (haja)
Analyze the key differences: necessity permits a prohibited act temporarily and only for the individual under duress; need does not contradict a text but violates general rules, and it establishes a permanent exception benefiting even those not in need.
Necessity is rare and compelling; need is ongoing and general.
3
Understand the author's critique and reformulation
Note that the author replaces 'special need' with 'general need' (haja 'amma) and defines it as encompassing the entire ummah or a significant group (e.g., a profession or region). The reformulation emphasizes the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj).
The citation from al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam supports the idea that public interest is like private necessity.
4
Apply the refined maxim to a scenario
Test the maxim: if a financial crisis affects all people in a region (general need), a ruling allowing a normally prohibited practice (e.g., a fee-based loan) may become permanently permissible. If only one individual faces hardship, the maxim does not apply; only the darura rule would temporarily permit relief.
The key is generality and permanence.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
ومزيَّةُ هذه الصيغة المعدلة أنها تُبرز مبدأ رفع الحرج، وهو من السمات الرئيسية في الشريعة الإسلامية من حيث هو الأساس في الأحكام الاستثنائية في حالة الضرورة الفردية، وهو نفسه الأساس هنا في باب الحاجة العامة .
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification
... for the community, unlike need, for it does not necessitate exceptional measures from general rulings except when it is the need of the community. That is because each individual has renewed needs different from others, and it is not possible to legislate specifically for each individual, unlike necessity, for it is a rare and compelling situation.

b) That the exceptional ruling which depends on necessity is a temporary permission of a prohibition forbidden by the text of Sharia; this permission ends with the removal of duress, and is restricted to the person under duress.
As for rulings established based on need, they do not contradict a text, but they contradict the rules and analogy, and they are established in a general and permanent manner, benefiting both the needy and others.
Thus need also differs from specific custom, because the ruling established by specific custom is limited to the people of that custom, as previously stated.

1007

Chapter Ten: General Rules in Fiqh
And the advantage of this amended formulation is that it highlights the principle of removing hardship, which is one of the main features of Islamic Sharia, as it is the basis for exceptional rulings in cases of individual necessity, and it is the same basis here in the chapter of general need.
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage is a juristic analysis and refinement of a rule drawn from the Majalla. The author first distinguishes between necessity (darura) and need (haja). Necessity permits a prohibited act temporarily and only for the individual under duress. Need, by contrast, does not clash with a textual prohibition but contradicts general rules and analogy; it establishes a general, permanent ruling that benefits anyone, not just the needy. The author then critiques the original formulation 'special need' (al-haja al-khassa), noting that it wrongly suggests individual need, while the intended meaning is the need of a group (e.g., a profession or region). He cites al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam's statement that public interest is like private necessity. The author proposes a reformulation: 'General need takes the place of private necessity' (al-haja al-'amma tunazzalu manzilat al-darura al-khassa), defining general need as that which encompasses the entire ummah or a subgroup. This refined maxim emphasizes the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj), which underpins both individual necessity exceptions and general need exceptions.

Key Terms
حاجة خاصة (hāja khāṣṣa): A need that is individual and not general; the author criticizes this term in the original rule as misleading.
حاجة عامة (hāja ʿāmma): A need that encompasses the entire nation (umma) or a significant group, such as the people of a craft or a region.
ضرورة (ḍarūra): A compelling situation that temporarily permits a prohibited act, restricted to the individual under duress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Necessity-based rulings are permanent, need-based are temporary
B) Necessity-based rulings are temporary and individual, need-based are general and permanent
C) Necessity-based rulings contradict texts, need-based do not
D) Both are temporary and individual

Key Takeaway
The refined maxim 'General need takes the place of private necessity' clarifies that only widespread, permanent needs justify exceptions from general rules, while individual emergencies fall under the temporary darura rule.

The Fifth Maxim: Custom is Authoritative (العادة محكمة) and Its Derived Rules
Introduces the fifth major maxim and lists 11 derived sub-maxims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'العادة محكمة', identify its conditions, and apply its 11 sub-maxims to new cases in contracts and interpretation.

Core Idea
Custom governs where texts are silent: consistent practice binds as law.

Custom (al-ʿādah / al-ʿurf) is a foundational source in Hanafi fiqh, especially in transactions and interpretation. This lesson introduces the fifth of the universal maxims, 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative), and its eleven derived sub-maxims. Through them, you will see how the Hanafi school harnesses custom to resolve ambiguity, determine obligations, and adapt rulings to changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its scope
The maxim 'العادة محكمة' means that established custom has binding legal force. It operates in all fiqh chapters but is particularly influential in contracts, oaths, and verbal expressions.
The author confirms that ‘al-ʿādah’ is synonymous with ‘al-ʿurf’ (custom) in both verbal and practical forms.
2
Learn the conditions for valid custom
Custom is only authoritative if it is consistent (muṭṭarid) or predominant (ghālib). Rare or irregular practices carry no weight. This is captured in sub-maxims B and C (38-39/81).
These conditions were discussed earlier in the theory of custom; the author does not repeat them here.
3
Explore the verbal custom sub-maxims
Sub-maxims D, E, and W (40-42/81) show that custom can override literal meanings, that writing equals speech, and that the customary gestures of a mute person are valid expressions.
They establish the authority of verbal custom in interpretation and communication.
4
Explore the practical custom sub-maxims
Sub-maxims Z, H, and T (43-45/81) establish that customary practices have the force of stipulated conditions, can designate beneficiaries, and that trade customs govern merchants.
These express the authority of practical custom, including special customs of merchants and craftsmen.
5
Apply the principle of changing rulings and giving effect to speech
Sub-maxim Y (46/81) allows rulings to evolve with time, while the sixth maxim (47/81) prioritizes giving effect to speech over ignoring it. Together, they show the dynamic nature of fiqh.
These maxims are essential for muftis responding to new situations.

Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh. The advantage of this modified formulation is that it highlights the principle of removing hardship, which is one of the main features of Islamic law as it is the basis for exceptional rulings in the case of individual necessity, and it is itself the basis here in the chapter of public need. 36/81 - The Fifth Maxim: Custom is authoritative (1) / M / 36). What is meant by al-ʿādah in this maxim is custom (`urf) in its two types: verbal and practical. It expresses the status of custom and its consideration in Islamic law, and its governing authority among people in distributing rights and obligations among them in transactions. This maxim and the following maxims derived from it have been previously cited and relied upon in several contexts of the theory of custom, sufficient to serve as an explanation and representation of its application, so there is no need to repeat that here. (See: V 9/67-69). What is derived from it of the maxims: 37/81 - A: The usage of people is a proof that must be acted upon (M/37). This maxim is like its original saying 'Custom is authoritative', and includes verbal and practical custom, because usage here means custom and custom absolutely. 38/81 - B: Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant (M/41). 39/81 - C: Consideration is given to the predominant and widespread, not the rare (M/42). These two maxims express some of the conditions for considering custom that were presented in the discussion of its theory, which are the condition of consistency or predominance, and they have been clarified there (see: V 2/70). (1) Muḥkamah with a shaddah on the kāf which is fatḥah, a passive participle from taḥkīm. Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majallah and Their Classification. 40/81 - D: The literal meaning is abandoned by implication of custom (M/40). 41/81 - E: Writing is like speech (M/69). 42/81 - W: The accustomed gestures of the mute person are like expression by tongue (M/ ). These three maxims lay down the foundations for considering verbal custom in speech and what stands in its place customarily in expression, such as writing and gestures. They have been previously cited in the theories of contracts and custom (see: V 6/30, 5-8/69). 43/81 - Z: What is known customarily is like what is stipulated as a condition (M/43). 44/81 - H: Designation by custom is like designation by text (M/45). 45/81 - T: What is customary among merchants is like what is stipulated among them (M/44). These three maxims express the authority of practical custom. The third of these lays down the established principle in considering special custom, such as the custom of merchants, and people of professions and crafts, and the people of a specific town, for it has a governing authority like general custom, but it is limited to those who share it, not others. An explanation of these maxims has also been presented in the theory of custom (see: V 6/69). 46/81 - Y: The change of rulings with changing times cannot be denied (M/39). Discussion of this maxim has been presented in detail at the end of the theory of custom (see: V/539). 47/81 - The Sixth Maxim: Giving effect to speech is better than ignoring it (M/60).
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on universal maxims in fiqh. It presents the fifth major maxim: 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative). The author clarifies that 'al-ʿādah' here means 'al-ʿurf' (custom) in both its verbal and practical forms. This maxim establishes that established customs and practices have legal force; they can determine meanings, distribute rights, and shape obligations. The author then lists eleven derived sub-maxims (furu') that expand on this principle: 1) 'استعمال الناس حجة يجب العمل بها' (the usage of people is a binding proof) – meaning that common practice is authoritative. 2) 'إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطّردت أو غلبت' (custom is only considered if consistent or predominant) – a condition for validity. 3) 'العبرة للغالب الشائع لا للنادر' (consideration is for the prevalent, not the rare). 4) 'الحقيقة تُترك بدلالة العادة' (the literal meaning is abandoned when custom indicates otherwise) – a key rule in interpretation. 5) 'الكتاب كالخطاب' (writing is like speech) – written words have the same binding effect as spoken ones. 6) 'الإشارات المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان' (customary gestures of a mute person are like verbal expression) – giving effect to non-verbal communication. 7) 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً' (what is known by custom is like a stipulated condition). 8) 'التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص' (designation by custom is like designation by text). 9) 'المعروف بين التجار كالمشروط بينهم' (what is customary among merchants is like a condition among them) – emphasizing trade customs. 10) 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (changes in rulings with changing times cannot be denied) – a crucial principle for ijtihad. 11) 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' (giving effect to speech is better than ignoring it) – the sixth maxim, which prioritizes operative meaning. The author notes that these sub-maxims have been discussed in earlier sections on the theory of custom, so he only summarizes them here.

Key Terms
العادة (al-ʿādah): Custom, synonymous with al-ʿurf; includes verbal and practical custom.
العرف (al-ʿurf): Custom or convention; divided into verbal (al-ʿurf al-lafẓī) and practical (al-ʿurf al-ʿamalī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'العادة محكمة', which of the following is a condition for a custom to be legally binding?
A) It must be ancient and unchanging.
B) It must be consistent (muṭṭarid) or predominant (ghālib).
C) It must be approved by the ruler.
D) It must be universally accepted in all Muslim lands.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'العادة محكمة' empowers custom to determine legal meanings and obligations, provided it is consistent or predominant and not contradicted by an explicit text.

The Maxim of Indivisible Entities in Hanafi Law
Stating a maxim with an application in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ذكر بعض مالا يتجزأ كذكر كله' to cases of indivisible legal acts and state the resulting ruling.

Core Idea
Mentioning part of an indivisible thing is like mentioning the whole – the effect applies to the entire entity.

This lesson introduces a concise legal maxim from the Majalla that governs how the law treats references to fractions of indivisible objects. Understanding this maxim is essential for muftis dealing with divorce and other indivisible acts, where partial pronouncements have full effect.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Commit the Arabic text of the maxim to memory: 'ذكر بعض مالا يتجزأ كذكر كله'. It is a sub-rule (dabt) under broader principles.
This maxim appears as Article 63 of the Majalla.
2
Understand Indivisibility
Identify what 'indivisible' means in a legal context: an entity that cannot be split into separate legal effects. Divorce (talaq) is a prime example.
Contrast with divisible acts like debt payment, where partial payment is possible.
3
Apply the Maxim to the Furu
Consider the given case: a man divorces half or a quarter of his wife. Apply the maxim: the entire wife is divorced because the wife is indivisible in the context of divorce.
The ruling does not depend on the husband's intent; the law imposes the full effect.
4
Recognize the Scope
Understand that this maxim applies only to indivisible entities. In Hanafi fiqh, acts like talaq, ulaq (oath of abstention), and certain contracts are treated as indivisible.
Other schools may treat some of these as divisible; the Hanafi position is strict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«ذكر بعض مالا يتجزأ كذكر كله» (م/٦٣). - فتطليق الرجل نصف امرأته أو ربعها مثلاً تطلق به كلها.
"Mention of something indivisible is like mention of the whole" (Majalla article 63). - So if a man divorces half of his wife or a quarter, for example, the entire wife is divorced by that.
Source pages p. 948

Commentary
This passage presents a concise legal maxim: when an indivisible entity is mentioned in part, the effect applies to the whole. Divorce (talaq) is an indivisible act; it cannot be applied fractionally. Hence, pronouncing divorce on a fraction of one's wife—e.g., 'half of my wife is divorced'— results in the entire wife being divorced, because the subject (wife) is indivisible in the context of divorce. This maxim is a sub-rule (dabt) under the broader principle that divorce cannot be partitioned. It emphasizes that the law does not give effect to impossible or partial intentions when the act inherently affects the whole.

Key Terms
مالا يتجزأ (mā lā yatajazza’): That which cannot be divided into legal parts; an indivisible entity in terms of legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'I divorce one-third of my wife,' what is the ruling according to this maxim?
A) Only one-third is divorced; the rest remains married.
B) The entire wife is divorced.
C) The divorce is invalid because it is ambiguous.
D) It depends on the husband's intent.

Key Takeaway
When dealing with indivisible legal acts, any reference to a fraction triggers the full effect; the maxim ensures legal certainty and prevents partial outcomes.

The Maxim: 'If Applying Speech is Impossible, It is Disregarded'
Legal maxim definition with explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, define its key terms, and apply it to determine when a statement must be disregarded after exhausting all interpretive possibilities.

Core Idea
Speech that cannot bear any valid meaning—literal or figurative—is legally disregarded.

This lesson examines a foundational legal maxim from the Majalla: 'If applying speech is impossible, it is disregarded.' The maxim sets the boundary where interpretive effort stops. Before discarding any statement, a jurist must exhaust all possible interpretations, including figurative ones. Only when no sound meaning can be assigned does the statement become legally irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim
State the Arabic maxim exactly: 'إذا تعذر إعمال الكلام يهمل'. Note the key terms: إعمال (application), تعذر (impossibility), يهمل (disregarded).
This maxim is Article 62 of the Majalla.
2
Define 'إعمال الكلام'
Understand that إعمال means the effort to give a valid legal meaning to a statement. This includes literal and metaphorical interpretations.
Hanafis prioritize إعمال over إهمال (disregard).
3
Define 'تعذر'
تعذر means impossibility. In this context, impossibility occurs when the statement cannot be interpreted correctly by any reasonable method, even figuratively.
If any plausible meaning exists, the statement must be applied.
4
Derive the ruling
When impossibility is established, the statement is disregarded (يهمل). It has no legal effect.
This prevents futile effort and maintains legal certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٤/٨١ - ز - «إذا تعذر إعمال الكلام يهمل» (م/ ٦٢).
التعذر في إعمال الكلام هو أن لا يمكن حمله على معنى صحيح ولو مجازاً، فيكون لغواً، فيهمل.
54/81 - Z - 'If applying speech is impossible, it is disregarded' (Majalla art. 62). The impossibility of applying speech is that it cannot be interpreted to a correct meaning even figuratively; then it becomes meaningless, so it is disregarded.
Source pages p. 949

Commentary
This passage states a maxim from the Majalla: if a statement cannot be given any valid interpretation—even a metaphorical one—it is legally ignored. The author clarifies that 'impossibility of application' means that no sound meaning can be attributed, whether literal or figurative. Such speech is considered futile (laghw) and disregarded. This prevents the legal system from wasting effort on nonsense. For example, a contract with contradictory terms that cannot be reconciled may be void, or an ambiguous utterance that defies interpretation may be treated as not said.

Key Terms
إعمال الكلام (i'māl al-kalām): The effort to give a valid legal meaning to a spoken statement, whether literal or figurative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim 'إذا تعذر إعمال الكلام يهمل,' what must a judge do before disregarding a statement?
A) Immediately disregard any ambiguous speech.
B) Try to give it a valid meaning, including figurative interpretation.
C) Ask the speaker to clarify.
D) Refer the matter to a higher court.

Key Takeaway
Speech that cannot be given any valid meaning—literal or figurative—is legally disregarded, but only after all interpretive efforts fail.

6 min
The Question is Repeated in the Answer
Defining a procedural maxim of acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-su'al mu'ad fi al-jawab' to determine the scope of an acknowledgment (iqrar) in judicial proceedings.

Core Idea
A simple 'yes' adopts all details of the question in legal acknowledgment.

Acknowledgment (iqrar) is a key evidence in Islamic courts. But what happens when the respondent simply says 'yes' to a detailed question? This lesson unpacks a vital procedural maxim from the Majalla that binds the answerer to every detail in the query.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
Recite the maxim 'السؤال معاد في الجواب' and understand that it means the answer legally repeats the question's details.
2
Identify the key components
The maxim involves two parts: the question (su'al) which contains details, and the answer (jawab) which uses a concise affirmative particle like na'am (yes) or bala (indeed).
The particle is the pivot that incorporates the prior context.
3
Understand the linguistic basis
These particles derive their meaning from the preceding statement; therefore, they carry all the specifics of the query.
This is a rule of Arabic semantics adopted in Hanafi jurisprudence.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider: 'Do you owe 500 dirhams to Zaid from a loan?' Answer: 'Yes.' The respondent is deemed to have acknowledged the full debt and its cause.
No need to repeat the words – the 'yes' suffices.
5
Note the condition and preventer
Condition: the question must be detailed and the answer unambiguous. Preventer: ambiguity or a caveat in the answer (e.g., 'Yes, but...') blocks full adoption.
If the answer is conditional, the maxim does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٣/٨١ - و - السؤال معاد في الجواب) (م/٦٦) . أي إذا وَرَدَ جواب بإحدى الأدوات المجملة مثل (نعم. ويلى) بعد سؤال مفصل يُعتبر الجواب مشتملاً على ما في السؤال من تفصيل، لأن مدلولات هذه الأدوات يعتمد على ما قبلها . فإذا سُئِل : هل لفلان عليك كذا وكذا بسبب كذا، فأجاب: نعم؛ أو سئل : أليس له عليك ،كذا فأجاب ،بلى كان مقراً بجميع ما ورد في السؤال، ولو لم يكرره.
53/81 - The question is repeated in the answer. That is, if an answer comes with one of the concise tools like 'yes' or 'indeed' after a detailed question, the answer is considered to include the details of the question, because the meanings of these tools depend on what precedes. So if he was asked: 'Is such-and-such owed to you by him for such-and-such reason?' and he answered 'yes'; or asked: 'Does he not have such-and-such upon you?' and he answered 'indeed', he is acknowledging all that was mentioned in the question, even if he did not repeat it.
Source pages p. 949

Commentary
This passage explains a procedural maxim from the Majalla (article 66). The maxim governs acknowledgments (iqrar) in court. When a detailed question is answered with a concise affirmative (like 'yes' or 'bala'), the answer is deemed to adopt all the details of the question. For example, if asked 'Do you owe X 100 dirhams from a loan?' and the defendant says 'Yes', it is an acknowledgment of the full debt and its cause, even if he did not repeat the words. This prevents evasion through incomplete answers. The maxim is based on the principle that these particles derive their meaning from context.

Key Terms
السؤال (al-su'al): The query containing detailed information that the answer adopts.
الجواب (al-jawab): The response, typically a concise affirmative particle.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or liability, legally binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone is asked: 'Did you borrow 500 from Khalid last year?' and answers 'Yes'. What is the ruling?
A) He acknowledged borrowing 500 last year.
B) He acknowledged borrowing but not the amount.
C) He acknowledged nothing because he didn't repeat.
D) The answer is invalid due to generality.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-su'al mu'ad fi al-jawab' ensures that a concise affirmative answer adopts all details of a detailed question in acknowledgments.

Ijtihad Is Not Overruled by Its Like: Finality of Ijtihadi Rulings
Qawida with furuat and istithna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-ijtihad la yanquduhu mithluh' to both self-changed ijtihad and differing ijtihad of another mujtahid, and identify the istithna' for clear error.

Core Idea
A previous ijtihad stands even if the mujtahid or another changes his view.

This lesson introduces a foundational qawida from the Majalla that protects the stability of ijtihadi rulings. Without it, no fatwa or judgment would ever be final. We will see how the rule operates in practice, including the famous precedent of Caliph Umar, and understand both its power and its sole exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida and its scope
Read the rule: 'Ijtihad is not overruled by its like.' Understand that it applies to any ijtihadi ruling—fatwa or judgment—across all fiqh chapters.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim governing ifta and qada.
2
Analyze the self-change scenario
The author gives the first furu': a mujtahid changes his ijtihad on a similar new case. The old ruling stands; only the new case is decided by the new view.
The rationale: otherwise no ruling would ever be settled.
3
Examine the cross-mujtahid scenario
The second furu': two mujtahids differ. Each must respect the other’s ijtihad; neither overrules the other once both are qualified.
This ensures pluralism within the madhhab and avoids anarchy.
4
Apply the exception (istithna')
If a ruling was based on a clear error against decisive evidence (qat'i), it may be overturned. This is not a case of legitimate ijtihad.
The qawida only protects valid ijtihad, not mistakes.
5
Connect to modern practice
The author draws a parallel to supreme courts: change in interpretation does not retroactively invalidate past decisions.
This shows the timeless legal principle behind the qawida.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
56/81 - القاعدة الثامنة : الاجتهاد لا ينقض بمثله» (م/16).
فمن اجتهد في حكم حادثة فأفتى بها أو قضى، ثم وقعت أخرى نظيرها فتبدل فيها اجتهاده إلى حكم ،مخالف لا تنقض فتواه أو قضاؤه السابقان وإنما يعمل باجتهاده الجديد في الحادثة الجديدة، إذ لو سَاغ نقض العمل بالفتاوى والأحكام الاجتهادية كلما تبدل اجتهاد المجتهد لما استقرَّ حكم في حادثة، لأنّ الاجتهاد عُرْضَةٌ للتبدل دائماً بتبدل وجهات النظر في الدليل.
وقد اشتهر عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه قضى في حادثة بحكم، ثم رفع إليه نظيرها فتبدل اجتهاده وقضى فيها بخلاف قضائه الأول، فقيل له في ذلك فأجاب: تلك على ما قضينا وهذه على ما نقضي». وقد جرت كلمته هذه مجرى الأمثال (ر) حاشية الحموي على الأشباه لابن نجيم تحت هذه القاعدة 141/1).
وهذا مبدأ قانوني مقرَّر أيضاً ؛ فإن محاكم النقض العليا، التي يعود إليها النقض والإبرام إذا تبدل اجتهادها القضائي في حادثة أو في فهم مادة قانونية لا يسري ذلك على ما مضى؛ فلا ينقض ما قد بُت فيه من قضايا وإنما يعمل باجتهادها الجديد في القضايا الجديدة.
وكما لا ينقض اجتهاد المجتهد بتبدل اجتهاده نفسه لا ينقض باجتهاد مجتهد غيره، بل على كل مجتهد أن يحترم اجتهاد سواه، لعدم المرجح
بعد تحقق رتبة الاجتهاد فيهما .
56/81 - The Eighth Rule: Ijtihad is not overruled by its like (Majalla art. 16). So whoever exercises ijtihad in a ruling on an incident and gives a fatwa or judgment, then another similar incident occurs and his ijtihad changes to a different ruling, his previous fatwa or judgment is not overruled; rather, his new ijtihad is acted upon in the new incident. For if it were permissible to nullify the operation of ijtihadi fatwas and rulings every time the mujtahid's ijtihad changed, no ruling on any incident would be settled, because ijtihad is always subject to change with changing perspectives on the evidence. It is well known from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he gave a judgment in one incident, then a similar one was referred to him and his ijtihad changed, and he ruled contrary to his first judgment. He was asked about that and replied: 'That was based on what we judged, and this is based on what we judge.' This saying became proverbial (see al-Hamawi's hashiyah on al-Ashbah of Ibn Nujaym under this rule, 1/141). This is also a settled legal principle; indeed, supreme courts of cassation, which have the power to quash or uphold, when their judicial ijtihad changes regarding an incident or understanding of a legal provision, does not apply retroactively; so what has been decided is not overturned, and they act on their new ijtihad only in new cases. And just as a mujtahid's ijtihad is not overruled by his own change of ijtihad, it is also not overruled by the ijtihad of another mujtahid. Rather, every mujtahid must respect the ijtihad of others, since there is no weight to prefer one over the other once both have attained the rank of ijtihad.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage presents a major qawida from the Majalla: 'Ijtihad is not overruled by its like.' It addresses the finality of ijtihadi rulings. The author first explains the rule: if a mujtahid changes his ijtihad on a similar new case, his previous fatwa or judgment stands; only the new case is decided by the new ijtihad. The reasoning is that otherwise no ruling would ever be final. He cites the famous example of Caliph Umar, who changed his ruling on a similar case and justified it with the phrase 'tilka 'ala ma qadayna wa hadhihi 'ala ma naqdi' (that was our former judgment, this is our new judgment). The author also extends the rule: one mujtahid's ijtihad does not overrule another's; each must respect the other's ijtihad. This is a cornerstone of Hanafi pluralism within the madhhab. The passage also draws a parallel to modern supreme courts: their change of interpretation does not retroactively invalidate past decisions.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent reasoning to derive a ruling from evidence; here, the process of a qualified jurist.
ينقض (yanqud): To overturn, invalidate, or nullify a previous ruling or fatwa.
بمثله (bi-mithlihi): By its like; meaning another ijtihad of the same type (same mujtahid or another of equal rank).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the qawida, if a mujtahid changes his ijtihad on a case similar to one he previously ruled on, what happens to his previous ruling?
A) It is automatically overruled.
B) It remains valid and is not overruled.
C) It becomes void unless the new ijtihad is the same.
D) It is referred to a higher authority.

Key Takeaway
A valid ijtihadi ruling cannot be overturned by a later ijtihad, whether from the same or a different mujtahid, unless based on clear error against decisive evidence.

The Ninth Rule: Exceptions to Qiyas Are Not Analogically Extended
Stating that rulings established as exceptions to qiyas cannot be analogically extended

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qawida, identify its scope, and apply it to distinguish between rulings that can and cannot be analogically extended.

Core Idea
Exceptions to qiyas are binding but not extendable; they remain confined to their specific evidence.

In Hanafi usul, many rulings are established through istihsan or specific texts that override the apparent analogy. This qawida, codified in Article 15 of the Majalla, acts as a critical brake: it prevents exceptions from being widened through further analogical reasoning. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when assessing whether a new case can be analogized from an exceptional ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its source
Memorize the Arabic wording: 'ما ثبت على خلاف القياس لا يقاس عليه'. Recognize that it is from the Majalla (Article 15) and widely accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
Also expressed as 'ما ثبت على خلاف القياس فغيره عليه لا يقاس'.
2
Define 'khilaf al-qiyas'
A ruling that contradicts what would be expected from strict analogy (qiyas) due to a specific text, necessity (darura), or juristic preference (istihsan).
Examples: salam (advance sale) is allowed despite the general prohibition of selling what you do not possess.
3
Recognize the limit imposed by the maxim
The fact that a ruling is an exception does not make it a precedent for other cases. You cannot use qiyas to extend that ruling to similar situations unless there is independent evidence.
This prevents the exception from becoming the norm.
4
Apply to furu' and test a hypothetical
Consider salam: allowed as khilaf al-qiyas. Can we apply qiyas to allow another advance contract (e.g., for services)? The maxim says no. Only if there is a specific text or general principle that also covers the new case.
Always verify the basis of the exception before attempting analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
57/81 - القاعدة التاسعة ما ثبَتَ على خلاف القياس لا يقاس عليه، أو ما ثبت على خلاف القياس فغيره عليه لا يقاس (م/ (15) .
57/81 - The Ninth Rule: What is established contrary to analogy is not subject to analogy, or: What is established contrary to analogy, other than it is not analogized to it (Majalla art. 15).
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage presents a concise qawida from the Majalla that limits the scope of analogical reasoning (qiyas). It states that if a ruling is established as an exception to the general rule of qiyas—meaning it was derived not by analogy but by another evidence (like a specific text, necessity, or istihsan)—then that ruling cannot be used as a basis for analogical extension to other cases. For example, the permissibility of salam (advance sale) is an exception to the prohibition of selling what one does not possess; it cannot be analogically applied to other contracts. This rule prevents the exception from becoming the norm. It is a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul and is often applied in mu'amalat.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on common 'illa.
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): A ruling that is an exception to the general analogical rule, often established by specific evidence or istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Salam (advance sale) is allowed despite the general prohibition of selling what you do not possess. According to this qawida, can you extend that ruling to other similar contracts by qiyas?
A) Yes, because the underlying reason is similar.
B) No, because it is established contrary to qiyas and cannot be extended.
C) Yes, always.
D) Only if the new contract is also for agricultural produce.

Key Takeaway
The maxim prevents the analogical extension of rulings that are exceptions to the general rule of qiyas, preserving the integrity of legal methodology.

The Maxim: What is Forbidden to Take is Forbidden to Give – Rationale, Applications, and Exceptions
States the maxim, provides rationale, lists prohibited acts, then exceptions and a derivative rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه', explain its rationale, apply it to bribery, riba, and wages for sinful acts, list its three exceptions, and state the derivative rule 'ما حرم فعله حرم طلبه'.

Core Idea
Giving what is haram to take makes you an accomplice in sin; necessity can permit the giver while the taker remains sinful.

This lesson introduces a key maxim in Hanafi legal ethics: if a thing is forbidden to acquire, it is equally forbidden to give it away. The reasoning is that giving facilitates and encourages the forbidden act, making the giver a partner. We will explore the rationale, classic examples, three exceptions, and a derivative rule that extends prohibition to seeking forbidden acts. This maxim is essential for muftis assessing the permissibility of transactions and payments in potentially sinful contexts.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Learn the Arabic maxim by heart: 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه'. Note its structure and clear implication.
The maxim appears in the Majalla as Article 60/81.
2
Understand the rationale
The author explains that giving assistance is encouragement, making the giver a partner in sin. Reflect on how this establishes moral responsibility for facilitation.
This aligns with Qur'anic prohibition of cooperation in sin (5:2).
3
Identify the main furuat
List the examples: bribery (rashwah), interest (riba), wages for wailing (niyahah), and wages for any sinful act. For each, both giving and taking are forbidden.
These are acts of disobedience that the maxim strongly prohibits facilitating.
4
Analyze the exceptions
Three cases where giving is allowed while taking remains prohibited: paying wicked persons/satirists when no authority, borrowing with interest under dire necessity, and an executor paying a usurper from orphan's property to save the rest.
The key is necessity to prevent greater harm or preserve assets.
5
Derive the subsidiary rule
From the maxim flows 'ما حرم فعله حرم طلبه': what is forbidden to do is forbidden to seek. This covers requesting, commanding, or otherwise soliciting the forbidden act.
This extends responsibility to indirect causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٠/٨١ - القاعدة الحادية عشرة : ما حَرُم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه» (م/ وذلك لأنَّ الإعطاء عندئذ إعانة وتشجيع على أخذ المحرم، فيكون المعطي شريك الآخذ في الإثم. وعلى هذا لا يجوز إعطاء الرّشوة والربا، والأجرة على النياحة، وعلى سائر الأعمال الفسقية، كما لا يجوز أخذ ذلك . الاستثناءات : ويستثنى من هذه القاعدة ما يُدفع للأشرار والهجائين للتخلص من أذاهم ولسانهم عند فقدان السلطان الوازع وما يدفعه المستقرض بالربا في حالة الاضطرار إلى الاستقراض، وما يدفعه الوصي من بعض مال اليتيم إلى غاصبه لتخليص الباقي إذا لم يمكن تخليصه دون ذلك . ففي جميع ذلك وأمثاله تكون الحرمة فيه على الآخذ دون المعطي. ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد ٦١/٨١ - ما حُرُم فعله حرم طلبه (م/ ٣٥) .
60/81 - The Eleventh Maxim: 'What is forbidden to take is forbidden to give' (Majalla article /). Because giving in that case is assistance and encouragement to take what is forbidden, so the giver becomes a partner in sin with the taker. On this basis, it is not permissible to give bribery (rishwa), interest (riba), wages for wailing (niyahah), and for all sinful acts, just as it is not permissible to take them. Exceptions: Excluded from this maxim is what is given to wicked people and satirists to get rid of their harm and tongue when there is no restraining authority, and what the borrower pays with interest in case of necessity to borrow, and what the executor (wasi) pays from some of the orphan's property to a usurper to save the rest if it cannot be saved without that. In all these and similar cases, the prohibition is on the taker, not the giver. Derivative rules from it: 61/81 - 'What is forbidden to do is forbidden to seek' (Majalla article 35).
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This passage discusses an important maxim that establishes the prohibition of facilitating sinful acts. The rationale is that giving something that is haram to take is a form of assistance in sin, making the giver a partner. Examples include giving bribes, paying or accepting interest, paying for wailing (a pre-Islamic practice), and wages for any sinful work. However, the author lists exceptions where giving becomes permissible while taking remains haram: paying off a tyrant or satirist to avoid harm when no authority prevents them, borrowing with interest under extreme necessity, and an executor paying a usurper from the orphan's property to recover the rest. In these cases, the sin lies with the taker. A derivative rule is then given: if doing something is forbidden, then seeking it (e.g., requesting or commanding it) is also forbidden. This is because seeking it leads to its occurrence. This passage is crucial in understanding the scope of indirect facilitation in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
الرشوة (al-rashwah): Bribery: giving something to a judge or official to obtain a favorable decision.
الربا (al-riba): Interest or usury: any stipulated excess in a loan or exchange of same commodities.
النياحة (al-niyāhah): Wailing: a pre-Islamic practice of hired mourners; receiving wages for it is forbidden.
إعانة (i'ānah): Assistance or facilitation; giving forbidden things is seen as i'ānah 'alā al-ma'siyah (assistance in sin).
اضطرار (iḍṭirār): Extreme necessity that overrides normal prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an exception to the maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه'?
A) Giving a gift to a friend.
B) Paying a surgeon for surgery.
C) Paying a tyrant to avoid his harm when no authority exists.
D) Paying wages for lawful work.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه' prohibits giving forbidden things, with exceptions for necessity and preservation of interests; the giver is excused while the taker remains sinful.
