6 min
The Maxim: Demanding a Forbidden Act is Forbidden
Explains a derivative maxim with an exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ما حرم فعله حرم طلبه' to identify when demanding a forbidden act is itself forbidden and identify the exception regarding judicial oaths.

Core Idea
Demanding a forbidden act is forbidden; but procedural rights like requesting an oath are exempt.

This lesson introduces a corollary maxim from the Hanafi legal tradition: if an act is haram, then demanding or requesting that act is also haram. This flows from the obligation to forbid evil. However, there is a key exception: a plaintiff may request a judicial oath from the defendant even if the plaintiff knows the defendant will lie. This exception preserves legal procedure without making the plaintiff sinful.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Write the maxim in Arabic and English. Understand that it means any act that is forbidden to perform is also forbidden to request or demand from another.
The maxim is from Majalla art. 35.
2
Understand the underlying rationale
The rationale is that silence about a forbidden act is not allowed; one must oppose it. Demanding it is a form of encouraging sin, which is even worse.
The text says: 'silence about the forbidden is not permissible legally; rather, it must be denounced and changed.'
3
Apply to an ordinary case
Consider ordering someone to commit a crime, like theft. The orderer is sinful and shares the punishment because he demanded a forbidden act.
This is the main furu' of the maxim.
4
Identify the exception
The exception is when a plaintiff requests the defendant to take a judicial oath, even if the plaintiff knows the defendant will lie. This is permissible.
The exception is based on the qawida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions).
5
Explain the exception's rationale
The oath is a legal right of the plaintiff; the sin of lying falls on the one who swears, not the one who requests. The plaintiff's intention is to secure his right, not to promote lying.
This preserves the integrity of legal proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما حُرُم فعله حرم طلبه (م/ ٣٥) .
لأن السكوت على المحرّم غير جائز شرعاً، بل يجب إنكاره وتغييره.
فطلبه محرم الاستثناءات :
كذلك بحكم الأولوية.
فتكليف الشخص غيره بفعل جريمة مثلاً يستحق به نصيباً من العقوبة. يستثنى من القاعدة جواز طلب المدعي تحليف خصمه اليمين مع
علمه بكذبه فيها .
What is forbidden to do is forbidden to demand (Majalla art. 35). Because silence about the forbidden is not permissible legally; rather, it must be denounced and changed. So demanding it is prohibited. Exceptions: By priority, likewise. So charging someone else to commit a crime, for example, deserves a share of punishment. Excepted from the rule is the permissibility of the plaintiff asking the defendant to take an oath even though he knows the defendant will lie under oath.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage presents a corollary maxim: if an act is forbidden, it is also forbidden to request or demand it. The reason is that remaining silent about a forbidden act is not allowed; one must oppose it. Demanding it is even worse. For example, ordering someone to commit a crime makes the orderer share in the punishment. An important exception is given: a plaintiff may request the defendant to take a judicial oath even if the plaintiff knows the defendant will lie, because the oath has legal effect and the plaintiff is entitled to it. This exception preserves the right to legal process.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath taken in court; the plaintiff can demand it even if he knows the defendant will lie.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, what is the ruling on demanding a forbidden act?
A) It is permissible if the demander does not actually perform the act.
B) It is forbidden (haram).
C) It is disliked (makruh) only.
D) It is permissible only if the demand is in writing.

Key Takeaway
The maxim forbids demanding what is forbidden, but procedural rights like requesting a judicial oath are exempt to preserve legal process.

Maxim 13: Finality of Voluntary Acts and Exceptions
Explanation of a legal maxim with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Whoever strives to nullify what was completed from his side, his striving is rejected' to identify which claims are barred and which exceptions apply in Hanafi mu'amalat.

Core Idea
If you voluntarily completed an act, you cannot undo it—unless it harms a minor, waqf, or public rights.

This lesson explores a powerful Hanafi maxim that enforces finality of voluntary legal acts. The maxim prevents a person from undermining their own prior actions, but with critical exceptions for the vulnerable. Through the Majalla's examples, you'll learn to apply this rule in practice as a mufti or legal analyst.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Read the Arabic text of the maxim and its English translation. Understand that it bars a person from revoking or challenging what they themselves have completed.
The maxim is a qa'ida wusta (intermediate) applicable to mu'amalat.
2
Analyze the furuat (applications)
Review each example: retraction of acknowledgment, estate division, fuduli claim, and guarantor's claim. For each, identify how the maxim applies to bar the claim.
Note the exception: a genuine fuduli who does not ratify the contract can claim – this is not a bar because the act was not 'from his side'.
3
Distinguish the istithna'at (exceptions)
Study the exceptions for minors, waqf, and public rights. Understand that gross fraud (ghubn fahish) or lack of capacity (e.g., waqf property) overrides finality.
These exceptions protect vulnerable parties and public interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٣/٨١ - القاعدة الثالثة عشرة : من سَعَى في نقض ما تم من جهته فسعيه مردود عليه» (م/ ١٠٠).
وعلى هذا لا يصح من المقرّ أن يرجع عن إقراره السابق بحجة الخطأ فيه .
ولو تقاسم الورثة التركة، ثم أدعى أحدهم أنها ملكه وأراد نقض القسمة لا تسمع دعواه المجلة (١٦٥٦) لأن إقدامه على المقاسمة هو إقرار ضمني بحقوق من قاسمهم فيها.
- ولو باع شيئاً أو اشترى ثم ادعى أنه كان فضولياً عن شخص آخر ولم يقبل بعقده يسمع منه هذا الادعاء .
- وكذلك لو كفل شخص بالدَرَك (١) لمشتري العقار، ثم جاء يطلب أخذه بالشفعة، أو يدعي ملكيته، لا يقبل منه .
الاستثناءات :
يستثنى من هذه القاعدة ما له مساس بحق قاصر أو وقف أو بحقوق الجماعة .
فالغبن الفاحش مثلاً في بيع مال القاصر، أو إيجار عقار الوقف لا ينفذ.
فلو باع الأب أو الوصي مال القاصر، أو أجر المتولي عقار الوقف، ثم ادعوا وقوع غبن فاحش فيه تسمع الدعوى منهم. - وكذا إذا اشترى شخص أرضاً ثم ادعى أن بائعها كان وقفها مسجداً (١) تقدم معنى الكفالة بالدَرَك، أو ضمان الدرك، فلينظر ٥/٤٦.
The thirteenth rule: Whoever strives to nullify what was completed from his side, his striving is rejected against him (Article 100). On this basis, it is not valid for a person who makes an acknowledgment to retract his previous acknowledgment on the grounds of error in it. If the heirs divide the estate, then one of them claims that it is his property and wants to nullify the division, his claim is not heard (Majalla 1656) because his undertaking the division is an implicit acknowledgment of the rights of those he divided with. - If he sold something or bought it, then claimed that he was an unauthorized agent (fuduli) for another person and does not accept the contract, this claim is heard from him. - Likewise, if a person guarantees against eviction (daman al-darak) for a buyer of real estate, then comes to claim preemption or to claim ownership, it is not accepted from him. Exceptions: Exempted from this rule anything that touches the right of a minor, a waqf, or the rights of the public. For example, gross fraud in the sale of a minor's property or the lease of waqf property is not effective. If the father or guardian sells the minor's property, or the administrator leases waqf property, then they claim that gross fraud occurred, their claim is heard. - Likewise, if a person buys land then claims that its seller had made it a mosque waqf (the meaning of kafala bi al-darak or daman al-darak is explained earlier; see 5/46).
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth legal maxim from the Majalla, which enforces finality of transactions and legal acts once they are voluntarily completed. The author provides several applications: an acknowledgment cannot be retracted on grounds of error; a division of estate cannot be undone by a participant who later claims ownership; a person who sells or buys as an actual party cannot later claim he was a fuduli (unauthorized agent) to nullify the contract; a guarantor against eviction cannot later claim preemption or ownership. The exceptions protect minors, waqf properties, and public rights—if gross fraud occurs, the transaction is voidable and claims are heard. This demonstrates Hanafi concern for balancing legal certainty with justice for vulnerable parties.

Key Terms
المقرّ (al-muqirr): A person who makes an acknowledgment (iqrar) of a right against himself.
الكفالة بالدرك (al-kafalah bi al-darak): A guarantee that the seller will indemnify the buyer if the sold property is evicted by a rightful claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim 'Whoever strives to nullify what was completed from his side, his striving is rejected', which of the following claims would be heard?
A) A person who made an acknowledgment of debt later claims it was a mistake.
B) A co-heir who participated in estate division later claims sole ownership.
C) A guardian who sold a minor's property claims there was gross fraud.
D) A guarantor against eviction later claims preemption rights.

Key Takeaway
This maxim upholds legal finality but yields when vulnerable parties or public rights are at stake.

Thirteenth Maxim: No Undoing What One Has Completed
Explaining the 13th maxim with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the thirteenth Hanafi maxim to cases involving confessions, estate divisions, and guarantees, and identify the exceptions for minors, waqf, and public rights.

Core Idea
You cannot undo what you yourself completed, unless it harms a minor, waqf, or public interest.

This lesson unpacks the thirteenth maxim from the Majalla, a corner stone of Hanafi legal reasoning. The maxim prevents a person from retracting or undermining their own prior legal acts, ensuring stability in transactions and acknowledgments. However, the law carves out exceptions to protect those who cannot protect themselves. A mufti must master both the rule and its carve-outs.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rationale
Read the maxim carefully: 'Whoever seeks to undo a matter that was completed by him, his effort is rejected against him.' The rationale is to prevent self-contradiction and to preserve the finality of legal acts that have been fully executed by the person himself.
The maxim applies only when the act was truly 'completed by him' (i.e., with full capacity and authority).
2
Apply to the given examples
Work through each furu given in the source: (1) a confessor cannot retract, (2) an heir who participates in division cannot later claim sole ownership, (3) a fuduli (unauthorized agent) can reject the contract because his original act was not completed by him with authority, (4) a surety who guarantees against eviction cannot later claim the property by pre-emption.
Note the distinction: in the fuduli case, the claim is heard because the act was not 'completed by him' in the full sense.
3
Identify the exceptions
The source lists three categories where the maxim does not apply: rights of a minor (qasir), waqf (endowment), and the community (gross fraud in sale of minor's property, lease of waqf, purchase of land secretly made mosque/cemetery). These override the maxim to prevent harm.
The governing qawida here is 'al-darar yuzal' (harm must be removed).
4
Determine the governing principle
The exceptions show that the maxim is not absolute; it is outweighed by the higher principle of removing harm, especially when vulnerable parties are involved. A mufti must weigh the maxim against the 'harm removal' principle.
This balancing act is typical in Hanafi jurisprudence: maxims are powerful but not unconditional.
5
Practice with a new case
Given a scenario (e.g., a person sells his car, then claims he was coerced), apply the maxim and decide if the claim is heard. If the coercion is proven and he had not completed the act freely, the exception might apply.
Always verify whether the original act was truly 'completed by him' with full legal capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
63/81 - القاعدة الثالثة عشرة : من سَعَى في نقض ما تم من جهته فسعيه مردود عليه» (م/ 100).

وعلى هذا لا يصح من المقرّ أن يرجع عن إقراره السابق بحجة الخطأ فيه .

ولو تقاسم الورثة التركة، ثم أدعى أحدهم أنها ملكه وأراد نقض القسمة لا تسمع دعواه المجلة (1656) لأن إقدامه على المقاسمة هو إقرار ضمني بحقوق من قاسمهم فيها.

- ولو باع شيئاً أو اشترى ثم ادعى أنه كان فضولياً عن شخص آخر ولم يقبل بعقده يسمع منه هذا الادعاء .

- وكذلك لو كفل شخص بالدَرَك (1) لمشتري العقار، ثم جاء يطلب أخذه بالشفعة، أو يدعي ملكيته، لا يقبل منه .

الاستثناءات :

يستثنى من هذه القاعدة ما له مساس بحق قاصر أو وقف أو بحقوق الجماعة .

فالغبن الفاحش مثلاً في بيع مال القاصر، أو إيجار عقار الوقف لا ينفذ.

فلو باع الأب أو الوصي مال القاصر، أو أجر المتولي عقار الوقف، ثم ادعوا وقوع غبن فاحش فيه تسمع الدعوى منهم. - وكذا إذا اشترى شخص أرضاً ثم ادعى أن بائعها كان وقفها مسجداً أو مقبرة تُسمع دعواه كل ذلك صيانة لحقوق القاصرين والجماعة. وإذا ثبت ذلك ينقض العقد من شرح والدي رحمه الله على القواعد).
63/81 - The thirteenth base: 'Whoever seeks to undo a matter that was completed by him, his effort is rejected against him' (Majalla article 100). Based on this, it is not valid for a person who has made an admission to retract his previous admission on the pretext of error in it. And if the heirs divided the estate, then one of them claimed it was his property and wanted to undo the division, his claim is not heard (Majalla 1656) because his proceeding with the division is an implicit acknowledgment of the rights of those with whom he divided. And if someone sold something or bought, then claimed that he was an unauthorized agent on behalf of another person and does not accept the contract, this claim is heard from him. And similarly, if a person gave a guarantee against eviction for the buyer of real estate, then came seeking to take it by pre-emption, or claiming ownership, it is not accepted from him. Exceptions: Excluded from this base is anything that touches the right of a minor, an endowment, or the rights of the community. For example, gross fraud in the sale of a minor's property or the lease of waqf property is not effective. So if the father or guardian sold the minor's property, or the administrator leased the waqf property, then they alleged that gross fraud occurred in it, their claim is heard. And likewise, if a person bought land then claimed that its seller had made it a mosque or cemetery, his claim is heard – all of this to protect the rights of minors and the community. And if that is proven, the contract is rescinded, from the commentary of my father (may Allah have mercy on him) on the rules.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth major maxim from the Majalla (article 100) and explains its application with several examples and exceptions. The passage type is a qawida with furuat (applications) and istithna'at (exceptions). The maxim states that anyone who attempts to undo a legal act that he himself has initiated and completed will have his attempt rejected. The first example: a person who confesses to something cannot later retract his confession claiming he was mistaken, because the confession was a complete act from him. The second example: if heirs divide an estate, one heir cannot later claim that the property was entirely his and seek to undo the division, because his participation in the division is an implicit acknowledgment of the others' shares. However, if someone sells or buys as an undisclosed agent (fuduli), he can later come forward and reject the contract, because his original act was not final. Also, a guarantor (surety) in a property sale cannot later claim the property by pre-emption or ownership, as that would be undermining his own guarantee. The exceptions protect vulnerable parties: minors, waqf (endowment) property, and public rights. For example, gross fraud in a minor's sale or a waqf lease is actionable; the contract can be rescinded if proven. Similarly, if someone buys land that was secretly a mosque or cemetery, he can claim to undo the sale. These exceptions ensure that the maxim does not harm those who need protection.

Key Terms
المقرّ (al-muqirr): The person who makes an admission or confession (iqrar) that has legal consequences.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized agent who acts on behalf of another without prior permission.
الكفالة بالدَرَك (al-kafalah bil-darak): A guarantee against eviction, i.e., the surety's responsibility if the sold property is reclaimed by a rightful owner.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fahish): Gross fraud or exploitation where the price is significantly above or below market value, typically over 5% for chattels or 20% for real estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the 13th maxim (من سعى في نقض ما تم من جهته فسعيه مردود عليه), which of the following scenarios would be an exception where the claim is heard?
A) A partner in a division of inheritance later claims the entire estate belonged to him.
B) A father sells his minor child's property and later alleges gross fraud.
C) A surety in a property sale later seeks to claim the property by pre-emption.
D) A person makes a confession and then claims it was a mistake.

Key Takeaway
The 13th maxim prevents retraction of one's own completed acts, but gives way to protect minors, waqf, and communal rights when harm is shown.

Fourteenth Maxim: Continuation is Easier than Initiation
Explaining the 14th maxim and its derivative with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'البقاء أسهل من الابتداء' to distinguish when a contractual deficiency is overlooked during continuation versus initiation.

Core Idea
Continuation is easier than initiation: contractual strictness applies at formation, not after.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim from the Majalla: continuation is easier than initiation. Mastery of this maxim allows you to evaluate when deficiencies in contracts are excused. We will explore its derivative rule and three applied cases from ijarah, hibah, and sale.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its derivative
Identify the two Majalla articles: the main maxim (article 56: 'البقاء أسهل من الابتداء') and its derivative (article 55: 'يغتفر في البقاء مالا يغتفر في الابتداء'). Understand that the derivative is the operational rule.
The analogy 'دفع أسهل من رفع' explains the logic.
2
Identify the scope
Recognize that this maxim primarily governs contracts and their conditions, especially in ijarah, hibah, and sale. It distinguishes between initiation (contract formation) and continuation (post-formation).
3
Work through the furuat
Analyze the three examples: (1) ijarah/hibah and subsequent commonality (overlooked), (2) sale by share at initiation (invalid), (3) sale then partial reclaim (valid for remaining).
Focus on timing: initiation vs continuation.
4
Apply the istithna'
Note that initial commonality in ijarah/hibah is NOT overlooked. The exception confirms the rule: leniency only for subsequent defects.
The istithna' reinforces the principle by showing the contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
64/81 - القاعدة الرابعة عشرة: البقاء أسهل من الابتداء) (م/(56).

كما أن الدفع أسهل من الرفع.

وعلى هذا يمكن أن يغتفر اختلال بعض شرائط العقود في حال استمرارها بعد وجودها، ولا يُغتفر ذلك في حال انعقادها.

ويتجلى ذلك في أمثلة القاعدة التالية المتفرعة عنها :

ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد

65/81 - يغتفر في البقاء مالا يغتفر في الابتداء» (م/ 55).

وذلك لأن البقاء أسهل، كما أفادته القاعدة السابقة :

- ومن أمثلتها ما تقدم من أن عقد الإجارة يشترط لصحته أن يكون المأجور متميزاً لا حصة شائعة وكذلك الهِبَة فيما يحتمل القسمة .

ولكن الفقهاء اغتفروا الشيوع الطارىء فيهما دون المقارن: (ر )) ف 9/25 - 10)، وقواعد الحمزاوي ص/ (205 لأن الطارىء يكون في حالة بقاء العقد دون ابتدائه. . وكذلك لو كان المجموعة أشياء ثمن مجمل، فاشترى شخص أحدها بحصته من ثمنها لم يصح لجهالة ثمنه عند العقد، وهذا يسمى: البيع بالحصة .

ولكن لو اشتراها كلها ثم استحق من يده بعضها، فله أن يرجع على البائع بثمن ما استحق ويبقى البيع في الباقي بحصته من الثمن. (انظر في معنى الاستحقاق ف 2/37).
64/81 - The fourteenth base: 'Continuation is easier than initiation' (Majalla article 56). Just as repulsion is easier than removal. Based on this, a deficiency in some conditions of contracts can be overlooked during their continuance after their existence, but it is not overlooked during their formation. This is manifested in the examples of the following base derived from it: what is derived from it of the rules. 65/81 - 'What is overlooked in continuation is not overlooked in initiation' (Majalla article 55). That is because continuation is easier, as the previous base indicated. And among its examples is what preceded: that the lease contract, for its validity, requires the leased item to be specific, not a shared portion; and similarly, hibah (gift) in something that is divisible. But the jurists overlooked subsequent arising commonality in them, but not initial commonality. And likewise, if there is a collection of items with a total price, and a person bought one of them for its share of the price, it is not valid due to ignorance of its price at the time of contract, and this is called 'sale by share.' But if he bought all of them, then some of them were reclaimed from his possession, he may recourse against the seller for the price of the reclaimed items, and the sale remains for the rest with its share of the price. (See the meaning of istihqaq in Fiqh 2/37).
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth major maxim (article 56) and its derivative (article 55) from the Majalla. The passage type is a qawida with its furu' (derivative) and examples. The core idea is that legal rules are more lenient for maintaining an existing contract than for establishing it. This is analogous to repulsion (دفع) being easier than removal (رفع): it is easier to prevent something from arising than to remove it once present. Therefore, certain conditions that are required at the inception of a contract may be waived if they arise later during the contract's duration. The derivative, 'What is overlooked in continuation is not overlooked in initiation' (article 55), applies this principle. Examples: In ijarah (lease) and hibah (gift), the subject matter must initially be specific and not a common share. However, if commonality arises later (e.g., co-ownership after the contract), it is overlooked. Another example: buying a single item from a set for a portion of the total price is invalid because the price is unknown at the time of contract. But if a person buys the entire set, and later part of it is reclaimed by a rightful owner, he can claim back the price for that part and keep the rest; this is allowed because the deficiency arises during the contract's continuation, not at inception.

Key Terms
الدفع أسهل من الرفع (ad-daf' ashal min ar-raf'): An analogy: repulsing something before it arises is easier than removing it after it exists.
اختلال شرائط العقود (ikhtilal sharai't al-'uqud): A deficiency or absence of some required conditions for a contract.
الشيوع الطارئ (ash-shuyu' at-tari'): Common ownership that arises after the contract's formation, as opposed to being present from the start.
البيع بالحصة (al-bay' bil-hissah): A sale of one item from a collection for its proportionate share of the total price, often invalid due to price uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'what is overlooked in continuation is not overlooked in initiation' (يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء), which is an example of something overlooked in continuation?
A) A lessee sublets without permission.
B) Common ownership that arises after the lease contract is made.
C) The leased item is destroyed before delivery.
D) The rent amount is unknown at the time of contract.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'continuation is easier than initiation' allows leniency for deficiencies that arise after a contract is formed, not at its inception.

Fifteenth Maxim: The Follower is a Follower
Stating the 15th maxim from the Majalla without elaboration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'التابع تابع', explain its meaning, and apply it to a concrete fiqh case identifying the tabi' and its matbu'.

Core Idea
Anything subordinate follows the ruling of its principal without separate mention.

The maxim 'التابع تابع' is one of the foundational legal principles in Hanafi fiqh that governs relationships of dependency. It means that anything subordinate to something else legally follows the ruling of that principal. This principle saves parties from having to list every accessory explicitly in contracts and provides a clear framework for inheritance and property law. In this lesson, you will memorize the maxim, understand its definition, and learn to apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Memorize the Arabic text 'التابع تابع' and its English translation 'The follower is a follower'. Recognize it as the fifteenth maxim from the Majalla (article 47).
This is a primary memorization anchor.
2
Define the key terms
Identify 'التابع' (at-tabi') as the subordinate entity and 'المتبوع' (al-matbu') as the principal. Understand that the tabi' is legally dependent on the matbu' and shares its ruling.
The term 'tabi'' covers accessories, conditions, and other dependents.
3
Identify the matbu' and tabi' in a given case
Given a scenario (e.g., sale of a house), determine which items are followers (fixtures, attached fittings) and which are independent. The matbu' is the house itself; the tabi' are its customary accessories.
Custom and explicit agreement determine what is a follower.
4
Derive the ruling
Apply the maxim: the ruling of the matbu' (sale of house) includes its tabi' (fixtures) without separate mention. The tabi' does not have an independent existence in that transaction.
The maxim applies unless the follower is explicitly excluded or custom dictates otherwise.

Source Text

66/81 - The fifteenth base: 'The follower is a follower' (Majalla article 47).
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth major maxim from the Majalla (article 47). The passage type is a qawida definition, stated here without examples or further explanation. The maxim 'التابع تابع' means that anything that is subordinate or ancillary to something else follows the ruling of the principal. In Hanafi fiqh, this applies in many areas: for example, the sale of a house includes the fixtures (followers) without separate mention; a condition that is part of a contract follows the contract's ruling. The maxim is a companion to other qawaid on dependency and is often applied in sales, inheritance, and property law.

Key Terms
التابع (at-tabi'): That which is subordinate or ancillary to something else; it follows the ruling of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The follower can act independently.
B) The follower is subject to its own rules.
C) The follower takes the ruling of its principal.
D) The follower is irrelevant in legal matters.

Key Takeaway
The follower (tabi') shares the ruling of its principal (matbu') by default, unless explicitly excluded or custom dictates otherwise.

Maxim: The Subsidiary Is Not Singled Out for a Ruling
Explaining the maxim's scope and applications with furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of the maxim 'التابع لا يفرد بالحكم', distinguish between integral and associated subsidiaries, and apply the maxim correctly to cases in contracts and torts.

Core Idea
A subsidiary that is an integral part cannot be independently contracted; but outside contracts, it can attract independent rulings.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal maxim 'التابع لا يفرد بالحكم' (the subsidiary is not singled out for a ruling). At first glance, the maxim seems to forbid any independent ruling on a subsidiary item. However, the passage clarifies that the maxim's actual scope is limited to integral subsidiaries in contractual settings. In torts (transgression), subsidiaries can be independent subjects of rulings, and non-integral accessories are not covered at all. Understanding this nuance resolves apparent contradictions and many exceptions listed by commentators.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its literal meaning
Begin by reading the Arabic maxim 'التابع لا يفرد بالحكم' and its English equivalent. Note that 'تابع' (subsidiary) can refer to any item that follows or is attached to another.
The maxim is ranked as a minor (sughra) sub-maxim (dabt) in the Hanafi scheme.
2
Identify the core scope: integral subsidiaries in contracts
The author asserts that the maxim applies only when the subsidiary is 'جزء أو كالجزء' (integral part or quasi-part) of the principal. In contracts, such a subsidiary cannot be the sole object of a transaction.
This is the key clarification that removes confusion.
3
Apply to sales cases (limb, fetus, milk, wool)
Analyze the furu' examples: selling a limb of a live animal, a fetus without its mother, milk in the udder, or wool on the back—all invalid because they are integral or inseparable at the time of contract.
Even if later possible (e.g., after slaughter), the contract is void.
4
Note the exception in torts (ghasb, jinayat)
In cases of transgression (e.g., usurpation, assault), the subsidiary can be singled out for rulings. For example, offspring of a usurped animal is not usurped itself, but if destroyed, the usurper must compensate. Similarly, a fetus is entitled to ghurra (blood-money) if miscarried due to assault.
The maxim does not apply to liability for harm; here the principle is 'الضرر يزال' (harm must be removed).
5
Distinguish non-integral accessories (key, halter)
Selling a key without its lock or a halter without its beast is valid because these are not integral parts of the principal item; they are merely associated. They can be contracted independently.
This distinction aligns the maxim with its correct scope and eliminates many alleged exceptions.

Source Text

Tenth Chapter: Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh: The ruling of this maxim is not limited to ownership of the substance, but also includes the right of disposition. If a broker offers what he was tasked to sell to a shopkeeper and leaves it with him, and the shopkeeper runs away with it, the broker is not liable because leaving it with him is a necessity of this sale, so it is permissible for the broker and he is not negligent. From the commentary of my father (may Allah have mercy on him) on the Maxims, quoting from Chapter 33 of Jami' al-Fusulain. 68/81-b: 'The subsidiary is not singled out for the ruling' (M/48). This maxim is confused in its application and branches because its wording appears more general than its subject, hence commentators mention many exceptions. a) What is meant by it, in my belief, is that the subsidiary that is of the type of part or like a part of another is not suitable to be a locus in contracts, i.e., specifically contracted upon. So selling a limb from a live sheep is invalid, even if slaughtered afterward and possible. Selling the fetus of a pregnant animal without its mother is invalid, and making it an exception in a sale is a corrupt condition. Similarly, milk in the udder and wool on the back of sheep are treated as such. b) Beyond this scope, the subsidiary can be singled out for rulings as in the case of transgression: If a usurped animal gives birth, its offspring is not considered usurped because the usurper did not remove the owner's possession from it, but merely came into his possession accidentally (ref: F 9/23). Similarly, if it perishes without his transgression or negligence, he does not guarantee it, but if he destroys it, he guarantees it. 1024: Chapter 81: Explanation of the Maxims of the Majalla and Their Classification. Similarly, if one strikes the belly of a pregnant woman and she miscarries a dead fetus, the striker guarantees its blood-money called 'ghurra', even though it was subsidiary to its mother. Likewise, they have established for the fetus a deficient capacity for obligations, by virtue of which it deserves four rights, including inheritance from its testator and bequest for it, provided it is born alive, as mentioned earlier in the theory of capacities (ref: F 4/60). It is permissible to sell a key without its lock, and a halter without its beast, even though both are considered subsidiaries that enter into the sale of the lock and beast respectively by inclusion without mention in the contract, because this maxim, as we have deduced, is specific to subsidiaries that are part or like part, in which case they are not suitable independently for contracting. With this understanding of this maxim, the confusion in its application is removed, as is the need for many of its exceptions, and it becomes consistent.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi legal maxim 'The subsidiary is not singled out for a ruling' (م/٤٨). The author begins by noting that the maxim applies not only to ownership of the substance but also to the right of disposition, illustrated with the example of a broker who leaves goods with a shopkeeper who absconds—the broker is not liable because the act of leaving the goods is incidental to the sale. The author then explains that the maxim is often misunderstood and has many exceptions. Its correct scope is limited to subsidiaries that are integral parts or quasi-parts of something else, which cannot serve as independent objects of contracts. Examples include selling a limb of a live animal (invalid), selling a fetus without its mother (invalid), and selling milk in the udder or wool on the back (invalid). However, outside the realm of contracts, in cases of tort or transgression (تعدي), the subsidiary can be subject to independent rulings: for instance, the offspring of a usurped animal is not considered usurped itself, but if the usurper destroys it, he must compensate. The author also discusses the fetus's capacity for inheritance and conditional bequest. Finally, he clarifies that selling a key without its lock or a halter without its beast is permissible because these items are not integral parts but merely associated objects; they can be contracted separately. This understanding resolves the seeming conflicts in the maxim's application.

Key Terms
التابع (al-tābi‘): A subsidiary that is part of another thing or like a part, such that it cannot be independently contracted upon
ملك العين (milk al-‘ayn): Ownership of the physical substance of property
ملك التصرف (milk al-taṣarruf): Right to dispose or manage property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'التابع لا يفرد بالحكم', which of the following sales is valid?
A) Selling a limb of a live sheep
B) Selling the fetus of a pregnant camel without its mother
C) Selling a key without its lock
D) Selling milk in the udder of a goat

Key Takeaway
The maxim only bars singling out integral subsidiaries in contracts; in torts, subsidiaries can have independent rulings, and non-integral accessories remain contractable.

Maxim: What Is Excused in Subsidiaries Is Not Excused in Others
Explaining the maxim through the waqf example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها' to distinguish between valid and invalid subsidiary endowments in Hanafi waqf law.

Core Idea
Legal conditions are strict for the principal; leniency is granted for its subsidiaries, but only when they accompany a valid principal.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi sub-maxim (dabt) that relaxes certain legal requirements for subsidiary items attached to a valid principal. The maxim is particularly important in the law of endowments (waqf), where immovability is a general condition but moveables can be validly endowed as subsidiaries of real estate. Understanding this distinction prevents unnecessary invalidations and ensures the integrity of the principal transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal and the subsidiary
Determine which item is the main locus (asl) of the transaction and which items are its subsidiaries (tawabi‘). In waqf, the principal is typically immovable property; subsidiaries are moveable contents like furniture, books, or livestock.
2
Check the condition on the principal
Verify that the principal meets all legal requirements for the transaction. In waqf, the principal must be immovable property (or otherwise valid by custom). If the principal is invalid, the maxim does not apply.
3
Apply the maxim to the subsidiary
If the principal is valid, the subsidiary is excused from the strict conditions (e.g., immovability) that would otherwise be required if it stood alone. The subsidiary benefits from the leniency because it follows the principal.
This leniency is limited to what is necessary for the subsidiary to accompany the principal.
4
Identify exceptions (custom)
Some subsidiaries, even if standing alone, are rendered valid by custom (e.g., books, funeral equipment). In such cases, the maxim is not needed, but it reinforces the result.
The maxim 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative) may override the general rule.

69/81-c: 'What is forgiven in subsidiaries is not forgiven in others' (M/54). Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'What is excused in subsidiaries is not excused in others' (م/٥٤). The author explains that the strict legal conditions required for the main object of a transaction must be fully satisfied in that principal, but leniency is granted for its subsidiary parts. The example given is from the law of waqf (endowment): the general rule is that a valid waqf requires the endowed asset to be immovable property (real estate); endowing moveables is invalid except by custom (e.g., books, funeral items). However, if one endows a real estate property like a village or a house along with its contents (moveables), the endowment of those moveables is valid as a subsidiary following the immovable. This illustrates how the maxim relaxes conditions for subsidiaries that accompany the principal.

Key Terms
يغتفر (yughfaru): To be excused or tolerated; leniency is shown.
التوابع (al-tawābi‘): Subsidiaries that accompany a principal in a transaction.
الشرائط الشرعية (al-sharā’iṭ al-shar‘iyya): Legal conditions required for the validity of a transaction.

D) Endowing a house without any moveables is invalid

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها' allows conditions to be relaxed for subsidiaries that attach to a valid principal, but the principal itself must meet all legal requirements.

Maxim: If the Principal Falls, the Subsidiary Falls
Explaining the maxim 'إذا سقط الأصل سقط الفرع' with the example of releasing a debtor and its effect on guarantor and pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إذا سقط الأصل سقط الفرع' to determine the effect of releasing a principal debtor on guarantors and pledges, and distinguish cases where the subsidiary is released independently.

Core Idea
When the principal falls, its accessories fall; but accessories can fall alone without affecting the principal.

This lesson introduces a key subsidiary maxim in Hanafi debt and security law. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly determining the effect of releasing one party in a multi-party obligation. The passage gives a clear example: releasing the debtor automatically discharges the guarantor and pledge, but releasing only the guarantor leaves the debt intact. We will unpack the reasoning and apply it to similar scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal and subsidiary
In any secured transaction, determine which obligation is primary (the debt) and which are accessory (guarantee, pledge).
Here the debtor is principal, guarantor and pledge are subsidiaries.
2
Apply the maxim to release of principal
If the creditor releases the principal debtor, the subsidiaries are automatically released. This is the direct operation of the maxim.
3
Check the reverse operation
The maxim states 'ولا عكس' – the fall of the subsidiary does not cause the principal to fall. So releasing the guarantor alone does not release the debtor.
This is the istithna.
4
Apply to new scenarios
Test other cases: if the creditor releases the pledge (e.g., returns the collateral), does the debt remain? Yes, by this maxim.
The subsidiary is independent as to its fall.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷۰/۸۱ - د - إذا سَقَطَ الأصل سقَطَ الفرع» (م/ ٥٠).
ولا عكس، أي لا يلزم من سقوط الفرع سقوط الأصل:
فلو أبرأ الدائن المدين برىء كفيله أيضاً وسقط رهنه إن كان الدين
موثقاً بكفيل أو رهن.
70/81-d: 'If the principal falls, the subsidiary falls' (M/50). And not the reverse, i.e., the fall of the subsidiary does not necessarily entail the fall of the principal: So if the creditor releases the debtor, his guarantor is also released, and his pledge is released if the debt was secured by a guarantor or pledge.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'If the principal falls, the subsidiary falls' (م/٥٠). The author clarifies that the converse does not hold: the fall of the subsidiary does not cause the principal to fall. The example given is from the law of debts: if a creditor releases (براءة) the debtor (the principal obligor), then automatically the guarantor (كفيل) and the pledge (رهن) are also released from liability, because they are subsidiaries to the debt. However, if the creditor releases only the guarantor, the debt itself remains binding on the debtor. This shows the one-way dependency: the subsidiary's existence is contingent on the principal, but the principal is not contingent on the subsidiary.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-aṣl): The principal (e.g., the debtor in a debt relationship)
الفرع (al-far‘): The subsidiary (e.g., guarantor or pledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The guarantor remains liable
B) The guarantor is also released
C) The guarantor becomes the primary debtor
D) The guarantor must be released separately

Key Takeaway
When the principal obligation falls, all subsidiary obligations fall automatically; but subsidiaries can be released independently without affecting the principal.

The Subsidiary Maxim in Hanafi Transactions
Maxim definition with waqf application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها' to identify when a subsidiary condition is relaxed in a transaction, and state the related istithna' (exception).

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the principal's strict conditions are relaxed for its accessories—unless custom overrides.

This lesson unpacks one of the subsidiary maxims (dawabit) from the Majalla: 'What is excused in subsidiaries is not excused in others.' As a mufti, you will often encounter transactions where the main object meets all conditions, but its accessories seem defective by themselves. This maxim provides the legal basis to uphold the subsidiary's validity, preserving the overall transaction from unnecessary nullification. We will examine its wording, scope, and a classic waqf illustration.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Write the Arabic maxim precisely: يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها. Note its rank: a subsidiary maxim (sughra), type dabt (restrictive), governing transactions.
Majalla Article 54.
2
Distinguish principal vs. subsidiary
Identify the principal (المحل الأصلي) as the main object of the transaction. The subsidiary (التابع) is an accessory that legally depends on the principal. The maxim relaxes conditions for the subsidiary only.
Example: In waqf, the house is principal; its furniture is subsidiary.
3
Identify the scope
Recognize that the maxim applies to all transactions (mu'amalat) where conditions attach to the principal object. It does not apply to ibadat unless there is a clear analogy.
4
Apply the fur' from the text
Take the waqf example: endowing real estate (principal) allows including movables (subsidiary) even though movables alone would not be valid waqf (except by custom). State the ruling: the subsidiary is valid.
5
Note the istithna'
The exception: if the subsidiary is taken alone (without principal), the strict condition applies. This is governed by the maxim 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative).
Books and funeral equipment are valid waqf by custom, not by this subsidiary maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

69/81 - J - 'What is excused in subsidiaries is not excused in others' (Majalla 54). That is, the legal conditions required in the place of transactions must all be present in the original principal, and they are relaxed in its subsidiaries. For example, in waqf, it is required that the waqf property be fixed property, i.e. real estate; waqf of movables is not valid except what is conventionally accepted like waqf of books and funeral equipment (see f 5/67). So it is not valid. But if one waqfs real estate like a village or a house including its movables, the waqf of these movables is also valid as subsidiary to the real estate.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage explains the maxim 'يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها' (What is excused in subsidiaries is not excused in others) from the Majalla (Art. 54). It is a subsidiary maxim (sughra) under the broad qawaid. The author defines it: the legal conditions required in transactions must be fully met in the principal object, but leniency is shown in its subsidiaries. Then he gives a fiqhi application: in waqf, the general rule is that only immovable property (real estate) can be validly endowed; movables are not permissible as waqf unless there is a custom to the contrary (e.g., books and funeral equipment). However, if one endows a real estate property (like a village or house) along with its contents (movables), the waqf of those movables is valid as a subsidiary following the principal. This illustrates the maxim: the strict condition of immovability is required for the principal but relaxed for its accessories.

Key Terms
توابع (tawābiʿ): Subsidiaries or accessories that are legally dependent on the principal in a transaction.
الشرائط الشرعية (al-sharāʾiṭ al-sharʿiyya): Legal conditions required for the validity of a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'يغتفر في التوابع ما لا يغتفر في غيرها' allows leniency for accessories in transactions, as illustrated by waqf where movables become valid when they accompany real estate.

If the Principal Falls, the Subsidiary Falls
Explaining a maxim about debt release and its effect on accessories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إذا سقط الأصل سقط الفرع' to determine the effect of releasing the principal obligation on its accessories in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
If the principal debt is extinguished, all its securities collapse with it.

This lesson explores a fundamental maxim from the Majalla that governs the relationship between a primary obligation (asl) and its accessories (far'), such as guarantees and mortgages. Understanding this maxim is crucial for muftis when advising on debt releases and their consequences. The maxim states that when the principal falls, the subsidiary falls, but crucially, the reverse is not true.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its converse
Read the maxim 'إذا سقط الأصل سقط الفرع' and note the exception 'ولا عكس'. Understand that the subsidiary's fall does not affect the principal.
This is a one-way dependency.
2
Identify principal and subsidiary
Identify the asl (e.g., debt) and the far' (e.g., guarantor, mortgage). The subsidiary exists only to secure the principal.
3
Apply to ibra' of debtor
Work through the example: the creditor releases the debtor. Since the principal debt falls, the guarantor is discharged and the mortgage is void.
This is the direct application of the maxim.
4
Apply to the converse case
Consider ibra' of the guarantor alone. The principal debt remains, so the debtor is still liable. The subsidiary can fall without touching the principal.
This is the istithna' – the exception that proves the rule.
5
Formulate the rule for practice
Summarize: 'When the principal obligation is extinguished, all accessories that depend on it are also extinguished. However, extinguishing an accessory does not affect the principal.'
Memorize this for fatwa application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷۰/۸۱ - د - إذا سَقَطَ الأصل سقَطَ الفرع» (م/ ٥٠). ولا عكس، أي لا يلزم من سقوط الفرع سقوط الأصل: فلو أبرأ الدائن المدين برىء كفيله أيضاً وسقط رهنه إن كان الدين موثقاً بكفيل أو رهن. بخلاف ما لو أبرأ الدائن الكفيل أو ردَّ الرهن فإن دين المدين لا يسقط .
70/81 - D - 'If the principal falls, the subsidiary falls' (Majalla 50). And not the reverse, i.e., the fall of the subsidiary does not necessitate the fall of the principal: So if the creditor releases the debtor, his guarantor is also discharged and his mortgage is also void if the debt was secured by a guarantor or mortgage. Contrary to if the creditor releases the guarantor or returns the mortgage, then the debtor's debt is not discharged.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage explains the maxim 'إذا سقط الأصل سقط الفرع' (If the principal falls, the subsidiary falls) from the Majalla (Art. 50). It is a subsidiary maxim. The author immediately notes the converse: the fall of the subsidiary does not imply the fall of the principal. The example given: if a creditor grants a release (ibra) to the debtor, the debt is extinguished, and consequently the guarantor (kafil) is discharged and any mortgage (rahn) is released. However, if the creditor releases only the guarantor or returns the mortgaged property, the debtor's obligation remains unaffected. This is because the guarantor and mortgage are subsidiary (far') to the principal debt (asl).

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The principal obligation or object in a legal relationship.
فرع (farʿ): The subsidiary that depends on the principal, e.g., guarantor, mortgage.
إبراء (ibraʾ): Release or discharge from a debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The guarantor's liability is reduced.

Key Takeaway
The fall of the principal obligation necessarily causes the fall of all accessories, but the fall of an accessory does not affect the principal.

The Subsidiary May Be Established Without the Principal
Explaining a maxim about proof in court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Majalla maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل', identify its scope (judicial proof, not reality), and apply it to cases involving confession (iqrar) as a limited proof.

Core Idea
In court, a subsidiary obligation may be proven on limited evidence even if the principal is not proven.

At first glance, the maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' seems illogical: how can a branch exist without a root? But the key is that this maxim deals with judicial proof, not physical reality. In legal proceedings, evidence may be available for the subsidiary (e.g., a guarantor) but not for the principal (the debtor). The confession of the subsidiary is a limited proof (ḥujja qāṣira) that binds only him. This lesson unpacks this counterintuitive yet essential principle of Islamic legal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish natural from legal existence
Recognize that the maxim does not claim a subsidiary can exist without a principal in reality; it only addresses what can be proved in court. In nature, a branch must have a root, but in law, evidence may be present for one and absent for the other.
2
Identify the domain: judicial proof
The maxim operates in the context of evidence before a judge. The court can only act on what is established by admissible proof. If only the subsidiary's liability is proven (e.g., by confession), the court holds him liable even if the principal's liability is unproven.
3
Recognize the key concept: limited proof (ḥujja qāṣira)
Confession (iqrār) is a limited proof: it binds only the confessor and does not extend to others. This is the mechanism that allows the subsidiary to be established without the principal. The confessor's statement is evidence against himself, not against third parties.
The passage states: 'al-iqrār ḥujja qāṣira, yasrī ʿalā ṣāḥibihi wa lā yasrī ʿalā ghayrihi.'
4
Apply to the example of guarantor (kafīl)
Consider a claim where A says B borrowed money and C guaranteed. C confesses, but B denies and there is no proof against B. The court cannot hold B liable, but C's confession establishes his guarantee. This does not mean a guarantee without a debtor; it means C's liability is proven by his own admission.
5
Apply to the example of acknowledging brotherhood
Similarly, if someone says 'this person is my brother,' he is affirming his own relationship. But this does not automatically establish the father's paternity. The father's denial or lack of proof means the brother's lineage to the father is not proven. However, the acknowledger is bound by his own statement (e.g., for inheritance obligations).
The passage is cut off but the principle is clear.

۷۱/۸۱ - هـ - قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل (م/۸۱). صيغة هذه القاعدة في المجلة: «قد يثبت الفرع مع عدم ثبوت الأصل.» هذه القاعدة تبدو غريبة غير معقولة في بادىء الرأي، لأنها تنافي السنن الطبيعية. ففي الطبيعة لا يوجد فرع بلا أصل. ولكن الأمور الحقوقية الاعتبارية تؤثر فيها عوامل تختلف عن العوامل الطبيعية. فهذه القاعدة تُعبر عن إثبات الحقوق أمام القضاء، ولا تبحث عن نشوئها في الواقع. فوجود الفرع يستلزم في الواقع وجود الأصل الذي تفرع عنه، ولكن إثبات المسؤوليات الحقوقية على الأشخاص قد تفقد وسائله المثبتة في حق الأصل، وتتوافر في حق الفرع. مثال ذلك : لو ادعى شخص على اثنين أنَّ أحدهما استقرض منه مبلغاً وأن الثاني قد كفله، فاعترف الكفيل، وأنكر الآخر: وعجز المدعي عن إثبات القرض عليه، يؤخذ المبلغ من الكفيل، لأن المرء مؤاخذ بإقراره كما سيأتي وليس هذا حكماً بأنه قد نشأت كفالة دون مدين مكفول، ولكن يد القضاء لا تمتد على شخص إلا بدليل ،مثبت، ولا دليل هنا إلا إقرار الكفيل والإقرار حجة قاصرة، فيسري على صاحبه ولا يسري على غيره كما سنرى. وكذا لو أقرَّ أحدٌ لشخص مجهول النسب أنه أخوه. فهذا الإقرار يمس حقوق الأب لأن فيه تحميلاً للنسب على الأب، فإنَّ كونه أخاً للمقر هو فرع عن بنوته لأبيه فيحتاج إلى تصديق الأب فإذا أنكر الأب بنوته ولم يمكن إثباتها بالبينة لا تثبت بنوته للأب، ولكن يؤاخذ المقر بإقراره أنه
71/81 - H - 'The subsidiary may be established without the principal' (Majalla 81). The wording of this maxim in the Majalla: 'The subsidiary may be established while the principal is not established.' This maxim appears strange and unreasonable at first glance, because it contradicts natural laws. In nature, there is no subsidiary without a principal. But legal and constructive matters are affected by factors different from natural factors. This maxim expresses the establishment of rights before the judiciary, and does not investigate their occurrence in reality. For the existence of the subsidiary necessarily implies in reality the existence of the principal from which it branched, but the establishment of legal responsibilities on persons may lose its means of proof with respect to the principal, while they are available with respect to the subsidiary. Example: If a person claims against two that one of them borrowed money from him and the second guaranteed him, and the guarantor confesses, while the other denies, and the claimant is unable to prove the loan against him, the amount is taken from the guarantor, because a person is held accountable by his confession as will come. This is not a ruling that a guarantee has arisen without a guaranteed debtor, but the hand of the judiciary does not extend to a person except with established evidence, and there is no evidence here except the guarantor's confession, and confession is a limited proof, so it applies to its owner and does not apply to others as we will see. Similarly, if a person acknowledges that someone of unknown lineage is his brother, this acknowledgment affects the rights of the father because it imposes lineage on the father, for his being a brother of the acknowledger is subsidiary to his being a son of the father, so it requires the father's confirmation. If the father denies his sonship and it cannot be proven by evidence, his sonship to the father is not established, but the acknowledger is bound by his confession that he...
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage explains the maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' (The subsidiary may be established without the principal) from the Majalla (Art. 81). The author notes its counterintuitive nature: in natural reality, a subsidiary cannot exist without a principal. However, in legal and judicial contexts, the issue is about proof, not actual existence. The maxim applies when evidence (like a confession) is available for the subsidiary but not for the principal. Example: if someone claims a loan against A, with B as guarantor, and B confesses but A denies, and no proof against A exists, B is still liable based on his confession. This does not mean a guarantee existed without a debtor; rather, the court can only act on available evidence. Another example: if someone acknowledges a person of unknown lineage as his brother, this cannot establish the brother's lineage to the father without the father's confirmation, but the acknowledger is held to his own statement, as it creates obligations for himself (e.g., inheritance?). The text is cut off but shows the principle.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Confession or acknowledgment that is binding only on the confessor.
حجة قاصرة (ḥujja qāṣira): A proof that is confined to its source and does not extend to others.
الفرع (al-farʿ): Subsidiary; in this maxim, the legal obligation or status that depends on a principal but can be proven independently.
الأصل (al-aṣl): Principal; the primary obligation or status from which the subsidiary derives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' applies in which scenario?
A) A property mortgage is proven even without the debt.
B) A guarantor is held liable based on his confession even though the debtor's liability is unproven.
C) A subsidiary is always independent of the principal.
D) A confession by one person always proves the liability of others.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' is a fundamental principle of evidence: a subsidiary can be judicially established through limited proof (like confession) even when the principal is not proven.

The Maxim: A Branch May Be Established Without the Root
Explains a maxim about judicial proof of branch without root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' to distinguish between judicial proof and substantive existence in cases involving admission (iqrar).

Core Idea
A branch can be proven in court even if the root is not proven—admission binds only the admitter.

This lesson introduces a maxim that at first seems unnatural: a branch can be established without its root. But in the legal world, proof is not the same as reality. The maxim operates in the realm of evidence, particularly when an admission (iqrar) binds a party. We will explore two concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh that clarify this principle and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim's apparent contradiction
Recognise that in nature, a branch always has a root. The maxim appears to deny this, but it concerns judicial proof, not physical existence.
The author warns: 'تبدو غريبة غير معقولة في بادىء الرأي' (seems strange and unreasonable at first sight).
2
Distinguish between substantive reality and evidentiary reality
The maxim applies only to what can be proven in court. A branch may exist in reality, but if the root is unproven, the branch can still be enforced against the person who admitted it.
Key concept: 'الإقرار حجة قاصرة' (admission is a limited proof).
3
Analyse the first example: guarantor's admission
A creditor claims a loan against a debtor and a guarantor. Only the guarantor admits. Because admission binds only the admitter, the guarantor is liable even though the debtor is not proven liable.
This does not mean the guarantee exists without a debtor; it means the court can only act on the available proof.
4
Analyse the second example: admission of brotherhood
Someone admits that a person of unknown lineage is his brother. This admission affects inheritance because the admitter is bound, even though the father's paternity is not proven.
The father's denial or lack of evidence does not release the admitter from his own admission.
5
Apply the maxim to new scenarios
Consider other situations where a branch liability might be proven without the root: e.g., an agent admitting receipt of funds without proof from the principal.
The key is that the admission or proof against the branch must be independent and conclusive against that party.

ولكن الأمور الحقوقية الاعتبارية تؤثر فيها عوامل تختلف عن العوامل الطبيعية. فهذه القاعدة تُعبر عن إثبات الحقوق أمام القضاء، ولا تبحث عن نشوئها في الواقع.
فوجود الفرع يستلزم في الواقع وجود الأصل الذي تفرع عنه، ولكن إثبات المسؤوليات الحقوقية على الأشخاص قد تفقد وسائله المثبتة في حق الأصل، وتتوافر في حق الفرع.
مثال ذلك : لو ادعى شخص على اثنين أنَّ أحدهما استقرض منه مبلغاً وأن الثاني قد كفله، فاعترف الكفيل، وأنكر الآخر: وعجز المدعي عن إثبات القرض عليه، يؤخذ المبلغ من الكفيل، لأن المرء مؤاخذ بإقراره كما سيأتي وليس هذا حكماً بأنه قد نشأت كفالة دون مدين مكفول، ولكن يد القضاء لا تمتد على شخص إلا بدليل مثبت، ولا دليل هنا إلا إقرار الكفيل والإقرار حجة قاصرة، فيسري على صاحبه ولا يسري على غيره كما سنرى. وكذا لو أقرَّ أحدٌ لشخص مجهول النسب أنه أخوه. فهذا الإقرار يمس حقوق الأب لأن فيه تحميلاً للنسب على الأب، فإنَّ كونه أخاً للمقر هو فرع عن بنوته لأبيه فيحتاج إلى تصديق الأب فإذا أنكر الأب بنوته ولم يمكن إثباتها بالبينة لا تثبت بنوته للأب، ولكن يؤاخذ المقر بإقراره أنه أخوه، فيقاسم ذلك الشخص حصته من ميراث الأب. ١٠٢٦
Chapter 10: Universal Legal Maxims. Unlike the case if the creditor releases the guarantor or returns the pledge, then the debt of the debtor does not drop. 71/81 (H) 3 A branch may be established without the root (M/81). The wording of this maxim in the Majalla: 'A branch may be established without the establishment of the root.' This maxim seems strange and unreasonable at first sight, because it contradicts natural laws. In nature, there is no branch without a root. But legal matters are conventional; factors different from natural factors influence them. This maxim expresses the establishment of rights before the judiciary, and does not investigate their actual origination. The existence of a branch necessitates in reality the existence of the root from which it branched, but proving legal liabilities against persons may lose its evidentiary means regarding the root and be available regarding the branch. An example: if a person claims against two that one of them borrowed a sum from him and the second guaranteed him, then the guarantor admitted and the other denied, and the claimant is unable to prove the loan against him, the sum is taken from the guarantor, because a person is held accountable for his admission as will come. This is not a ruling that a guarantee has arisen without a guaranteed debtor, but the hand of the judiciary does not extend to any person except with a proven proof, and there is no proof here except the guarantor's admission, and admission is a limited proof; it applies to its own party and does not apply to others as we will see. Likewise, if a person admits concerning a person of unknown lineage that he is his brother, this admission affects the rights of the father because it imposes lineage on the father. For his being a brother to the admitter is a branch of his being a son to the father, so it needs the father's confirmation. If the father denies his sonship and it cannot be proved by evidence, his sonship to the father is not established, but the admitter is held liable by his admission that he is his brother, so he shares that person's share of the father's inheritance.
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'A branch may be established without the root' (Majalla article 81). The author begins by noting that this maxim appears counterintuitive from a natural perspective, but clarifies that it operates in the realm of judicial proof, not physical reality. The maxim applies when there is procedural evidence (such as an admission) against the branch but not against the root. Two examples illustrate the principle: (1) If a creditor claims a loan against a debtor and a guarantor, and only the guarantor admits, the guarantor is held liable because his admission binds him, even though the debtor's liability is not proven. This does not mean the guarantee exists without a debtor—it means the court can only act on available proof. (2) If someone admits to a person of unknown lineage that he is his brother, this affects inheritance because the admitter is bound by his admission, even though the father's paternity cannot be proven. The maxim is a rule of evidence, not a statement about substantive reality.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far'): The branch; something that derives from another (the root) in the legal context, such as a guarantor's liability deriving from the debtor's liability.
الأصل (al-asl): The root; the primary right or obligation from which the branch derives, such as the debtor's principal debt.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or confession; a legal proof that binds only the person who makes it, not others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل', why is the guarantor held liable even though the debtor is not proven liable?
A) Because the guarantee is independent of the debtor's liability.
B) Because the guarantor's admission is binding on him, even though the debtor's liability is not proven.
C) Because the creditor can choose to sue the guarantor instead of the debtor.
D) Because the maxim allows courts to ignore the need for proof against the principal.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'قد يثبت الفرع دون الأصل' teaches that in judicial proof, a derivative right or obligation can be established against a party based on their own admission, even if the primary right (root) is not proven.

Maxim: If a Thing Is Void, Its Contents Become Void
Explaining the maxim with three applications and one exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'If a thing is void, its contents become void' (Majalla 52) to furu' cases and identify when the exception based on legislative purpose overrides the maxim.

Core Idea
When a contract falls, so do its conditions — except when the law's purpose says otherwise.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi legal maxim (qa'ida sughra) from the Majalla. Through three concrete applications — contract conditions, settlement consideration, and built-upon releases — you will see how the maxim operates. You will also learn a critical exception: the shuf'a right, where legislative intent overrides the general rule.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
The maxim 'If a thing is void, its contents become void' applies to contracts and legal acts. Understand that it only comes into play when the container (e.g., contract) is void ab initio.
The variant 'If the contained is void, the container becomes void' emphasizes the same relationship from the opposite direction.
2
Work through the furu' cases
Apply the maxim to each case: void contract → void conditions; void settlement → void consideration; void sale → void release. Note the subsidiary relationship.
The third case shows that what is 'built upon' the contract also falls, not just what is 'contained'.
3
Identify the exception
The shuf'a case: settlement for pre-emption right is void, but the right is dropped without compensation. Why? Because the law's purpose is to prevent harm, not to generate profit.
This exception is governed by the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their purposes).
4
Reflect on the overriding principle
The exception teaches that legal maxims are not absolute; they yield to higher legislative intent. The mufti must always consider the maqsad behind the rule.
This is a key lesson in iftaa: balancing general principles with specific purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

72/81 - And: 'If a thing is void, what is within it becomes void' (Majalla Art. 52). Similar is their saying: 'If the contained is void, the container becomes void' (with active voice in the first and passive voice in the second). So if a contract is void, the conditions and obligations contained in it become void, because they are subsidiary to it. And if someone makes a settlement (sulh) with his opponent who claimed against him for a consideration, then after the settlement the claimant admits that he had no right against him, the settlement is void, so what it contained of transferring the consideration becomes void, thus the person who made the settlement (musalih) has the right to recover it. Likewise, just as the content of the contract becomes void with its voidness, also what is built upon it becomes void. For example, if two parties made a sale and the exchange of the sold item and the price took place, and each of them released the other from every right and claim related to this sale, then the sold item is reclaimed from the buyer's hand, then he [the buyer] recovers the price from the seller, because when the sale became void due to the reclamation of the sold item, the [mutual] release built upon it became void. Based on this, the text of the maxim should be stated: 'Void is what is within it and what is built upon it.' Exceptions: This maxim has exceptions, including: if the pre-emptor (shafi') settles for his right of pre-emption (shuf'a) in the sold real estate for a consideration, the settlement is not valid, but his pre-emption falls without consideration, because the right of pre-emption was legislated to be exercised actually to repel bad neighborliness, not to be exploited. So here the settlement became void, but what it contained of dropping the pre-emption did not become void, because his consent to dropping his pre-emption for a consideration is evidence that in his view there is no bad neighborliness.
Source pages p. 962

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim 'If a thing is void, its contents become void' (Majalla Art. 52), along with a variant 'If the contained is void, the container becomes void.' The author provides three applications: first, if a contract is void, all its conditions and obligations fall because they are subsidiary; second, if a settlement (sulh) is made but the claimant admits no right existed, the settlement is void and the consideration must be returned; third, if a sale is rescinded due to a defect (istihqaq), the mutual release (ibra) built upon that sale also becomes void. The author also notes that the maxim should include 'what is built upon it' as well. An important exception is given: if a pre-emptor (shafi') enters a settlement to drop his right of pre-emption for compensation, the settlement is invalid, yet the pre-emption right is dropped without compensation because the purpose of shuf'a is to prevent harm, not to be a financial tool. This shows that the maxim does not apply universally; the intent of the law may override the general rule.

Key Terms
بطل (batula): To become void; null and void from the beginning (i.e., no legal effect).
ضمن (damn): What is contained within something; the contents or subsidiary elements.
صلح (sulh): A settlement contract between disputing parties for consideration.
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption in real estate to prevent harm from a bad neighbor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'If a thing is void, its contents become void', if a pre-emptor (shafi') settles for his pre-emption right for a consideration, what happens?
A) The settlement is valid, and the pre-emption right is dropped with compensation.
B) The settlement is void, and the pre-emption right remains.
C) The settlement is void, but the pre-emption right is dropped without compensation.
D) The settlement is valid, but the pre-emption right remains.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'If a thing is void, its contents become void' governs contracts and settlements, but it is overridden when the law's purpose (maqsad) requires otherwise, as in the shuf'a exception.

Applying the Maxim: When the Original Is Impossible, Recourse Is Had to the Substitute
Explaining the maxim with two applications in ghasb and sale defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إذا تعذر الأصل يُصار إلى البدل' to cases of ghasb and sale defects, and construct a fatwa consultation identifying when a substitute is required.

Core Idea
When the original obligation is impossible, a substitute (mithl/qimah) replaces it.

This lesson unpacks the sixteenth legal maxim from the Majalla: 'When the original is impossible, recourse is had to the substitute.' The maxim governs situations where the primary obligation (e.g., returning a usurped item or a defective sold item) cannot be performed. Through two practical applications—ghasb and the option of defect in sales—you will learn how Hanafi jurists shift from the original to the substitute, whether a similar item (mithl) or its value (qimah).

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the core rule: when performing the original is impossible, shift to the substitute.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qawida, derived from the broader principle of preventing harm.
2
Analyze the first application: ghasb
Examine the ruling for usurpation: the usurper must return the original 'ayn; if it perishes, they return a substitute (mithl or qimah).
The type of substitute depends on the nature of the item: mithliyyat require mithl, qimiyyat require qimah.
3
Analyze the second application: sale of defective goods
Study the scenario where the buyer alters the item (e.g., dyes a garment) then discovers a pre-existing defect. Returning the item is impossible, so the buyer claims the proportional difference in price based on the defect's reduction in value.
The calculation method is given: evaluate item as sound and as defective, then reduce the price proportionally.
4
Identify the trigger condition
Pinpoint when the original becomes impossible: either physical destruction (ghasb) or legal prohibition after alteration (sale defect).
Impossibility must be genuine—not merely inconvenient.
5
Apply the maxim to a new case
Practice by constructing a similar scenario (e.g., mithli vs. qimmi items) and determine the appropriate substitute.
Remember to distinguish between mithl and qimah per Hanafi classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷۳/۸۱ - القاعدة السادسة عشرة : إذا تعذر الأصل يُصار إلى البدل» (م/ ٥٣).
- فيجب رد عين المغصوب ما دامت قائمة. فإذا هَلَكَ يرد بدله من مثل أو قيمة .
- وإذا تعذر رد المبيع المعيب لمانع ،شرعي، كما لو كان المبيع ثوباً فصبغه المشتري، ثم ظهر فيه عيب ،قديم فللمشتري أن يسترد من البائع فرق نقصان العيب .
وطريقة تحديد هذا الفرق تكون بتقويم المبيع سليماً ومعيباً، فما ينقصه العيب من قيمة السليم ينقص بنسبته من الثمن فلو كان الثمن المتفق عليه في العقد خمسين، ثم قدرت قيمة المبيع سليماً بمائة ومعيباً بخمسين كان فرق العيب خمسين في المائة من القيمة، أي نصفها. فيسترد نصف الثمن أي خمس وعشرون .
73/81 - The sixteenth maxim: 'When the original is impossible, recourse is had to the substitute' (Majalla Art. 53). So the actual usurped property must be returned as long as it exists. If it perishes, its substitute is returned, of the same type or value. And if returning the defective sold item is prevented by a legal obstacle, such as if the sold item was a garment and the buyer dyed it, then a pre-existing defect appears, then the buyer has the right to recover from the seller the difference of the defect. The method of determining this difference is by evaluating the sold item sound and defective. Whatever the defect reduces from the sound value is reduced proportionally from the price. So if the agreed price in the contract was fifty, and the value of the sold item was estimated as sound at one hundred and defective at fifty, the defect difference is fifty percent of the value, i.e., half of it. So he recovers half the price, i.e., twenty-five.
Source pages p. 963

Commentary
This passage explains the sixteenth maxim, 'When the original is impossible, recourse is had to the substitute' (Majalla Art. 53). The maxim applies when the primary obligation cannot be fulfilled, requiring a substitute. The author gives two applications: first, in the case of usurpation (ghasb), the original property must be returned; if it is destroyed, the usurper must return a similar item or its value. Second, in a sale where the buyer alters the item (e.g., by dying a garment) and then discovers a pre-existing defect, returning the item is not possible. Instead, the buyer can claim the difference in value caused by the defect, calculated proportionally from the price based on the reduction in value. This demonstrates that impossibility of returning the original triggers a substitute in the form of compensation.

Key Terms
تعذر (ta'adhdhara): To be impossible or difficult to perform.
أصل (asl): The original thing or primary obligation.
بدل (badal): Substitute; a like item (mithl) or value (qimah) to replace the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'When the original is impossible, recourse is had to the substitute', what must the usurper do if the usurped property perishes?
A) Return the original if possible.
B) Pay a fine to the owner.
C) Return a substitute of the same type or value.
D) Nothing, because the property is gone.

Key Takeaway
The maxim triggers a substitute (mithl or qimah) when returning the original is legally impossible, ensuring fairness in ghasb and sale defects.
