Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
6 min
الساقط لا يعود: What Has Fallen Does Not Return
Explaining the maxim with three applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'الساقط لا يعود', explain its meaning, and apply it to the three furūʿ of debt release, seller's retention, and easements, and to a novel fatwa case.

Core Idea
Once a right is legally dropped, it is permanently extinguished and cannot be revived.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi legal maxim 'What has fallen does not return' (الساقط لا يعود), codified as Article 51 of the Majalla. The maxim establishes the finality of acts that relinquish rights. Understanding this maxim is essential for muftis and jurists when assessing whether a dropped right can be claimed again. We will explore its definition and three practical applications from classical fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its definition
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note that the maxim is called a 'musqit' (disconnector) — it severs the right permanently.
The maxim is general: any right that falls due to a legal cause becomes non-existent and cannot return.
2
Understand the first furuʿ: creditor releases debtor
If a creditor releases (ibrāʿ) the debtor, the debt falls and cannot be restored even if the creditor regrets it.
This is a standard application in loan and release contracts.
3
Understand the second furuʿ: seller's right of retention
If the seller delivers the sold item before receiving payment, the right to retain (ḥaqq al-ḥabs) the item falls permanently. The seller can only sue for the price.
This illustrates the maxim in sale transactions.
4
Understand the third furuʿ: easement rights
If the owner of a dominant tenement drops an easement right (e.g., passage or drainage), it is gone and cannot be reclaimed.
This shows the maxim applies to incorporeal rights as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٤/٨١ - القاعدة السابعة عشرة: الساقط لا يعود» (م/٥١).
مسقط أي أن ما يسقط من الحقوق بسبب يصبح بسقوطه معدوماً فلا يعود، كما لا يعود المعدوم.
فلو أبرأ الدائنُ مدينهُ سَقَط الدين، فلا تمكن استعادته إذا ندم الدائن. - ولو سَلَّم البائعُ المبيعَ للمشتري قبل قبض الثمن سقط حقه في احتباس المبيع لأجل استيفاء الثمن، فلا يعود له حق استرداد المبيع لأجل احتباسه بعد ذلك، وإنما له ملاحقة المشتري بالثمن كما تقدم (ر: ف ٥١ ه و ۱۲/۸۱)
- وكذا حق الارتفاق بالمرور أو المسيل لعقار في أرض الغير أو داره لو أسقطه صاحب العقار المرتفق سقط وليس له الرجوع فيه .
74/81 - The seventeenth maxim: 'What has fallen does not return' (Majalla Art. 51). It is a disconnector, i.e., whatever of rights falls due to a cause becomes non-existent by its falling, so it does not return, just as the non-existent does not return. So if the creditor releases his debtor, the debt falls, and it cannot be restored if the creditor regrets it. And if the seller delivers the sold item to the buyer before receiving the price, his right to retain the sold item for collecting the price falls, and his right to reclaim the sold item for retention thereafter does not return; rather he has the right to pursue the buyer for the price, as previously mentioned (see: f 51 h and 12/81). Similarly, a right of easement (irtifaq) for passage or drainage for a property on another's land or house, if the owner of the dominant tenement drops it, it falls and he has no recourse to it.
Source pages p. 963

Commentary
This passage explains the seventeenth maxim, 'What has fallen does not return' (Majalla Art. 51). The maxim means that once a right is legally dropped or forfeited, it ceases to exist and cannot be revived. The author provides three applications: first, if a creditor releases a debtor, the debt falls and cannot be reclaimed if the creditor changes his mind; second, if the seller delivers the sold item before receiving the price, his right to retain (habs) the item is lost and cannot be restored—he can only sue for the price; third, if the owner of a dominant tenement drops an easement right (e.g., passage or drainage), it is gone and cannot be reclaimed. This emphasizes the finality of legal acts that relinquish rights.

Key Terms
ساقط (saqit): What has fallen or been dropped; a right that has been extinguished.
مسقط (musqit): A cause that drops a right; the text says it is 'مُسقط' meaning a disconnector.
حق الحبس (haqq al-habs): The seller's right to retain the sold item until payment is received.
ارتفاق (irtifaq): An easement; a right of use over another's property (e.g., passage, drainage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'What has fallen does not return', if a seller delivers the sold item to the buyer before receiving payment, what happens to the seller's right to retain the item?
A) The right remains and can be exercised later.
B) The right returns once the buyer delays payment.
C) The right falls and cannot return; the seller can only sue for the price.
D) The right is transferred to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'الساقط لا يعود' establishes that once a right is legally dropped, it is permanently extinguished and cannot be revived.
